If you just look at the solution, for example, then tell yourself. Oh yeah, I see **why `they` did that**. Then the solution is not really yours. *You've done almost nothing to knit those concepts into your own underlying neural circuitry*. **Merely glancing at a solution and thinking you truly know it yourself is one of the most common illusions of competence in learning**. You must have the information persisting in your memory if you're to master the material well enough to do well on tests and to think creatively with it.

因此，`你`是解决问题或掌握概念的关键，而不是写了解答手册或书籍的人。如果你只是看解答，然后对自己说：“哦，我明白`他们`**为什么这么做**了”，那么这个解答并不真正属于你。你*几乎没有做任何工作将这些概念编织到自己的神经回路中*。**仅仅浏览答案并认为自己已经掌握它，是学习中最常见的能力错觉之一**。要想在考试中表现出色并创造性地运用所学知识，你必须将信息持久地存储在记忆中。

In a related thing, **you may be surprised to learn that highlighting and underlining must be done very carefully. Otherwise it can not only be ineffective, but also misleading**. It's as if, making lots of motions with your hand can fool you into thinking you've placed the concept in your brain. If you do mark up the text, try to look for main ideas before making any marks. And try to **keep your underlining or highlighting to a minimum**. One sentence or less per paragraph. On the other hand, **words or notes in a margin that synthesize key concepts are a very good idea**. Jeff Karpicke, the same researcher who's done such important work related to recall, has also done research on a related topic. `Illusions of competence` in learning. *The reason students like to keep rereading their notes or a textbook, is that when they have the book or Google open right in front of them, it provides the illusion that the material is also in their brains. But it's not, because it can be easier to look at the book instead of recalling, students persist in their illusions studying in a way that just isn't very effective.*

另外，你可能会惊讶地发现，**做标记和划线需要非常小心，否则它不仅无效，还可能具有误导性**。大量的手部动作可能会让你误以为已经将概念记在了脑海中。如果确实要标记文本，先找出主要思想再做标记，尽量**保持标记或划线的最小化**，每段最多标记一句话。另一方面，**在页边空白处写下对关键概念的总结是非常好的方法**。卡皮克博士，这位在回忆方面做出重要研究的学者，也在相关主题上进行了研究，即学习中的`能力错觉`。*学生们之所以喜欢重复阅读笔记或教科书，是因为当书本或谷歌就在面前时，提供了一种材料也在大脑中的错觉。但事实并非如此，因为查看书本比回忆更容易，学生们坚持以这种低效的方式学习。*

This is a reminder that just wanting to learn the material, and spending a lot of time with it, doesn't guarantee you'll actually learn it. **A super helpful way to make sure you're learning and not fooling yourself with illusions of competence, is to `test` yourself on whatever you're learning.** In some sense, **that's what `recall` is actually doing**. **Allowing you to see whether or not you really grasp an idea**. If you make a mistake in what you are doing, it's actually a very good thing. You want to try not to repeat you mistakes, of course, but **mistakes are very valuable** to make in your little self-tests before high stakes real tests. *Because they allow you to make repairs and you're thinking flaws bit by bit mistakes help correct your thinking, so that you can learn better and do better.*

这是一个提醒，仅仅想学习材料并花大量时间并不保证你真的学会了。**一种确保你真正学习而不是欺骗自己的能力的有效方法，就是`测试`自己所学的内容**。从某种意义上讲，这**也是`回忆`**在做的事，**让你看到自己是否真正理解了一个想法**。如果在学习中犯错，实际上是件好事。当然你要尽量避免重复错误，但在高风险考试前的小测试中**犯错非常有价值**，*因为它们可以让你逐步修正思维中的错误，从而帮助你更好地学习并表现更出色*。

As you know now `recall` is a powerful tool. But here's another tip, **recalling material when you are outside your usual place of study can also help you strengthen your grasp of the material**. You don't realize it, but when you are learning something new you can often take in subliminal cues for the room and the space around you at the time you were originally learning the material. This can throw you off when you take tests because you often take tests in a room that's different from the room you were learning in. By recalling and thinking about the material when you are in various physical environment, you **become independent of the cues from any one given location**. That helps you avoid the problem of the test room being different from where you originally learned the material. I'm Barbara Oakley, thanks for learning about learning.

现在你已经知道`回忆`是一种强大的工具，但这里还有另一个建议：**在不同于平时学习的地方回忆材料也可以帮助你加强对材料的掌握**。你可能没有意识到，当你学习新事物时，你可能会无意识地吸收周围房间和空间的暗示。这会在考试时影响你，因为你通常会在不同于学习的房间里考试。通过在不同环境中回忆并思考材料，你可以**摆脱任何特定位置的暗示**，从而避免考试房间与学习房间不同的问题。我是芭芭拉·奥克利，谢谢你学习有关学习的内容。

### 2.6 What Motivates You?

Welcome back! **It is hard to learn when you're not into it**. But if it's something you're really interested in, learning is easy. Why is that? *Most of the neurons in your cortex carry information about what is happening around you and what you're doing. Your brain also has a set of diffusely projecting systems of `neuromodulators` [ˌnjʊərəʊˈmɒdjʊleɪtə], that carry information not about the content of an experience but its importance and value to your future, neuromodulators are chemicals that influence how a neurons responds to other neurons.* And today we will discuss three of them. `Acetylcholine`  [ˌæsɪtɪl'kɒli:n], `Dopamine` [ˈdəʊpəmiːn] and `Serotonin` [ˌsɪərəˈtəʊnɪn].

欢迎回来！**当你不感兴趣时，学习会很困难**。但如果是你真正感兴趣的事情，学习就变得轻松了。为什么会这样？*你大脑皮层中的大多数神经元传递的是你周围发生的事情和你在做的事情的信息。你的大脑还拥有一组广泛投射的`神经调质`系统，这些系统传递的不是体验的内容，而是它对未来的重要性和价值。神经调质是一些化学物质，它们会影响神经元对其他神经元的反应。*今天我们将讨论其中的三种：`乙酰胆碱`、`多巴胺`和`血清素`。

`Acetylcholine neurons` form `neuromodulatory connections` to the `cortex` that are particularly important for focused learning, **when you are paying close attention. These acetylcholine neurons project widely and activate circuits that control synaptic plasticity.** Leading to new long term memory. Neuromodulators also have a profound impact on your unconscious mind. One of the great brain discoveries in my life time, has been that **our motivation is controlled by a particular chemical substance called `Dopamine`**. *Which is found in a small set of neurons in our brain stem shown here in orange. These dopamine neurons are part of a large brain system that controls reward learning and in particular in the basal ganglia, which is located in the green region above the dopamine neurons and below the cortex at the top of the brain. Dopamine is released from these neurons, when we receive an unexpected reward. Dopamine signals project widely and have a very powerful effect on learning. And this is something that also affects decision-making. And even the value of sensory inputs.*

`乙酰胆碱神经元`在`皮层`中形成`神经调质连接`，对**集中的学习**特别重要:**当你专注时，这些乙酰胆碱神经元广泛投射，并激活控制突触可塑性的回路，导致新的长期记忆的形成。**神经调质也对你的潜意识有深远的影响。我一生中最重要的脑科学发现之一是，**动机是由一种叫`多巴胺`的化学物质控制的。***这些多巴胺神经元位于脑干的一个小区域，负责奖励学习，尤其是在基底神经节（位于多巴胺神经元上方的大脑绿色区域）。当我们获得意外的奖励时，多巴胺从这些神经元释放出来。多巴胺信号广泛投射，对学习有很强的影响，这也会影响决策以及感官输入的价值。*

`Dopamine` is **in the business of predicting future rewards and not just the immediate reward. This can motivate you to do something that may not be rewarding right now but will lead to a much better reward in the future**. Addictive drugs artificially increase dopamine activity and fool your brain into thinking that something wonderful has just happened. In fact just the opposite has just happened. This leads to craving and dependence, which can hijack your free will and can motivate actions that are harmful to you. Loss of `Dopamine neuron`s leads to a *lack of motivation; and something called anhedonia, which is a loss of interest in things that once gave you pleasure*. Severe loss of Dopamine neurons causes resting tremor, slowness, rigidity, this is called Parkinson's disease. Ultimately it leads to catatonia, a complete lack of any movement. Dopamine neurons are part of the unconscious part of your brain that you learned about in the first week. **When you promise to treat yourself something after a study section you are tapping into your dopamine system**.

`多巴胺`的作用**不仅在于预测未来的奖励，还能激励你去做一些现在可能不立即有回报的事情，但将来会有更好的回报**。成瘾性药物会人为增加多巴胺活动，欺骗你的大脑，以为有了美妙的体验，实际上恰恰相反。这会导致渴望和依赖，甚至会劫持你的自由意志，驱使你去做对自己有害的行为。`多巴胺神经元`的缺失会导致*缺乏动机，甚至出现快感缺乏症，即对曾经让你愉悦的事物失去兴趣*。严重缺失会导致静止性震颤、迟缓和僵硬，即帕金森病。最终可能发展为紧张症，即完全缺乏任何动作。**当你在学习后奖励自己时，其实就是在激活你的多巴胺系统**。

`Serotonin` is a third diffuse neuromodulatory system that strongly affects your `social life`. In monkey troops the alpha male has the highest level of serotonin activity and the lowest ranking male has the lowest levels. `Prozac`, which is prescribed for clinical depression, raises the level of Serotonin activity. The level of Serotonin is also closely linked to `risk taking behavior`. With higher risk in lower Serotonin monkeys. Inmates in jail for violent crimes have some of the lowest levels of serotonin activity in society.

`血清素`是第三种扩散性神经调质系统，它对你的`社交生活`有很大影响。在猴群中，首领雄性拥有最高的血清素活动，而地位最低的雄性拥有最低的血清素水平。`百忧解（Prozac）`是一种治疗抑郁症的药物，它可以提高血清素的活性。血清素水平还与`风险行为`密切相关，血清素水平较低的猴子风险行为更高。因暴力犯罪而入狱的犯人通常有社会中最低的血清素活动水平。

Finally **your emotions strongly affect learning as you are well aware**. Emotions were once thought to be separate from cognition but recent research has shown that emotions are intertwined with perception and attention and **interact with learning and memory**. The `amygdala` [ə'mɪɡdələ] an almond shaped structure shown here, nestled down at the base of the brain is one of the **major centers where cognition and emotion are effectively integrated**. The amygdala is part of the `limbic system` which together with `hippocampus` is involved in **processing memory and decision making** as well as regulating emotional reactions. **You will want to keep your amygdala happy to be an effective learner**. The emotions and your neuromodulatory systems are slower than perception and action but are no less important for successful learning.

最后，**你的情绪对学习有着强烈的影响**。情绪曾被认为与认知分离，但最新研究表明情绪与知觉和注意力交织在一起，并**与学习和记忆相互作用**。`杏仁核`是一个位于大脑底部的杏仁状结构，是**认知和情绪有效整合的主要中心**之一。杏仁核是`边缘系统`的一部分，和`海马`体一起参与**处理记忆、决策以及调节情绪反应**。**你需要保持杏仁核的愉悦，才能成为一个有效的学习者**。情绪和神经调质系统的反应速度虽比知觉和行动慢，但同样对成功学习至关重要。

If you want to learn more about Acetylcholine, Dopamine, and Serotonin, look them on www.brainfacts.org, a website that is filled with valuable facts about your brain. I'm Terry Sejnowski. Happy learning, until we meet again.

如果你想了解更多关于乙酰胆碱、多巴胺和血清素的知识，请访问www.brainfacts.org，这是一个充满大脑知识的网站。我是特里·塞伊诺夫斯基，祝你学习愉快，下次再见！

### 2.7 The Value of a Library of Chunks

The ability to combine chunks in new and original ways underlies a lot of historical innovation. Bill Gates and other industry leaders set aside extended week-long reading periods so that they can hold many and varied ideas in mind during one time. *This helps generate their own innovative thinking by allowing fresh in mind not yet forgotten ideas to network amongst themselves.* Basically what people do to enhance their knowledge and gain expertise is to gradually build the number of chunks in their mind. Valuable bits of information, they can piece together in new and creative ways. Chess masters, for example, can easily access thousands of different chess patterns. Musicians, linguists and scientists can each access similar chunks of knowledge in their own disciplines. *The bigger and more well practiced your chunked mental library, whatever the subject you're learning, the more easily you'll be able to solve problems and figure out solutions.* As we'll discover soon, chunking isn't all you'll need to develop creative flexibility in your learning. But it's an important component. **Chunks can also help you understand new concepts.** This is because when you grasp one chunk, **you'll find that that chunk can be related in surprising ways to similar chunks, not only in that field, but also in very different fields.** This idea is called `transfer`. For example, concepts and problems solving methods you learned for physics can be very similar to chunked concepts in business. I've found some aspects of language learning were very helpful for me when I later began to learn Computer Programming.

将块状信息以新颖的方式组合在一起是许多历史创新的基础。比尔·盖茨和其他行业领袖会专门抽出一周的时间来阅读，使他们能够在同一时间内记住许多多样化的想法。这*有助于激发他们自己的创新思维，因为它允许新鲜、尚未被遗忘的想法在脑中相互联系*。基本上，人们通过逐渐在大脑中积累块状信息来增强知识并获得专业技能。它们是有价值的信息片段，可以被创意性地拼接在一起。例如，国际象棋大师能够轻松调用成千上万种不同的棋局模式。音乐家、语言学家和科学家也都可以在各自的学科中访问类似的块状知识。*无论你学习的主题是什么，你的块状信息库越大、练习得越熟练，解决问题和找到解决方案就越容易。*正如我们将要发现的，块状信息并不是你在学习中培养创造性灵活性的唯一要素，但它是一个重要组成部分。**块状信息还可以帮助你理解新概念**。这是因为，当你掌握一个块时，你**会发现这个块可以以令人惊讶的方式与类似的块相关联，不仅是在该领域内，还可以跨领域进行。**这种思想被称为`迁移`。例如，你在物理中学到的概念和问题解决方法可能与商业中的块状概念非常相似。我发现，学习语言的一些方面在我后来学习计算机编程时非常有帮助。

**A chunk is a way of compressing information much more compactly.** `As you gain more experience in chunking in particular subject, you'll see that the chunks you're able to create are bigger.` In some sense, that the ribbons are longer. Not only are those ribbons longer but the neural patterns are in some sense darker. *They're more solid and firmly ingrained. If you have a library of concepts and solutions internalized as chunked patterns, you can think of it as a collection or a library of neural patterns. When you're trying to figure something out, if you have a good library of these chunks, you can more easily skip to the right solution by metaphorically speaking, listening to whispers from your diffuse mode.* **Your diffuse mode can help you connect two or more chunks together in new ways to solve novel problems. Another way to think of it is this. As you build each chunk, it is filling in a part of your larger `knowledge picture`.** *But if you don't practice with your growing chunks, they can remain faint, and it's harder to put together the big picture of what you're trying to learn.* **In building a chunked library, you're training your brain to recognize not only a specific concept, but different types and classes of concepts so that you can automatically know how to solve quickly or handle whatever you encounter. You'll start to see `patterns that simplify problem solving` for you and will soon find that different solution techniques are lurking at the edge of your memory.** Before midterms or finals it can be easy to brush up and have these solutions at the mental ready.

**块状信息本质上是一种更紧凑地压缩信息的方式**。*随着你在特定学科中积累更多的块状经验，你会发现你能够创建的块变得更大。*某种意义上，它们就像更长的丝带。而且这些丝带不仅更长，*它们的神经模式在某种意义上也更深、更牢固。如果你有一个内化为块状模式的概念和解决方案库，你可以把它看作是一个神经模式的集合或图书馆。当你尝试解决问题时，如果你有一个良好的块状信息库，你就能更容易地跳到正确的解决方案，就像是隐约听到发散模式的低语。***发散模式可以帮助你以新的方式将两个或更多块连接在一起，以解决新问题。换个角度来看，当你构建每一个块时，它填补了你更大`知识图景`的一部分。***但是，如果你不练习逐渐增长的块状信息，它们可能会保持模糊，使你更难拼凑出你试图学习的整体图景。***在构建块状信息库的过程中，你是在训练你的大脑去识别不仅是特定概念，还有不同类型和类别的概念，这样你就可以自动知道如何快速解决或处理你遇到的任何情况。你会开始看到`简化问题解决的模式`，并很快发现不同的解决技巧在你的记忆边缘显现。**在期中考试或期末考试之前，复习这些解决方案会变得更容易，它们随时准备好供你使用。

**There are two ways to figure something out or to solve problems. First there's sequential step by step reasoning, and second through a more holistic intuition.** *Sequential thinking, where each small step leads deliberately towards a solution, involves the focused mode.* Intuition on the other hand often seems to require this creative diffuse mode linking of several seemingly different focused mode thoughts. **Most difficult problems and concepts are grasped through intuition, because these new ideas make a leap away from what you're familiar with.** Keep in mind that *the diffuse mode's semi-random way of making connections means that the solutions they provide should be very carefully verified using the focused mode. Intuitive insights aren't always correct.*

**解决问题有两种方式：一种是按顺序一步步推理，另一种是通过更整体的直觉。***按顺序的思维中，每个小步骤都有目的地指向解决方案，这涉及到专注模式。*而直觉往往需要这种创造性的发散模式，将几种看似不同的专注模式想法联系起来。**大多数困难的问题和概念是通过直觉掌握的，因为这些新想法需要跳出你熟悉的范畴。**然而，要记住，*发散模式以半随机的方式建立联系，这意味着它们提供的解决方案应通过专注模式仔细验证。直觉洞察并不总是正确的。*

You may think *there are so many problems and concepts, just in a single section or chapter of whatever you're studying, there's just no way to learn them all.* This is where the `law of serendipity` comes into play. `Lady luck favors the one who tries.` *Just focus on whatever section you're studying. You'll find that once you put that first problem or concept in your mental library, whatever it is, then the second concept will go in a little more easily. And the third more easily still.* Not that all of this is a snap, but it does *get easier*.

你可能会觉得，在你学习的任何章节或部分中*有太多的问题和概念，几乎无法全部掌握*。这时，`偶然性定律——“幸运女神眷顾努力的人”`——就起作用了。*专注于你正在学习的部分，你会发现，一旦你将第一个问题或概念放入你的心智库中，不管它是什么，第二个概念会更容易进入。第三个会更加容易。*这并不是说这整个过程一帆风顺，但它确实会变得*越来越轻松*。

I am Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning about learning.

我是芭芭拉·奥克利。感谢你学习如何学习。

### 2.8 Overlearning, Chocking, Einstellung, and Interleaving

*When you're learning a new idea, for example a new vocabulary word or a new concept or a new problem solving approach, you sometimes tend to practice it over and over again during the same study session. A little of this is useful and necessary, but continuing to study or practice after you've mastered what you can in the session is called `overlearning`. Overlearning can have its place. It can produce an `automaticity` that can be important when you're executing a serve in tennis or a perfect piano concerto. If you choke on tests or public speaking, overlearning can be especially valuable.* You know that even expert public speakers practice on the order of 70 hours for a typical 20-minute TED Talk? *`Automaticity` can indeed be helpful in times of nervousness, but be wary of repetitive overlearning during a single session. Research has shown it can be a waste of valuable learning time. The reality is, once you've got the basic idea down during a session, continuing to hammer away at it during the same session doesn't strengthen the kinds of long term memory connections you want to have strengthened. Worse yet, focusing on one technique is a little like learning carpentry by only practicing with a hammer. After a while you think you can fix anything by just bashing at it.*

*当你学习一个新概念，比如一个新词汇、一个新概念或一个新的问题解决方法时，有时会倾向于在同一个学习阶段反复练习。适度的重复是有益和必要的，但在你已经掌握了这一阶段内容之后，继续学习或练习称为`过度学习`。过度学习在某些情况下是有价值的，它可以产生一种`自动性`，这在网球发球或完美的钢琴协奏曲时非常重要。如果你在考试或公众演讲时感到紧张，过度学习尤为重要*。你知道，即使是专家级的演讲者，通常也会为一个20分钟的TED演讲练习约70个小时吗？**在紧张的时候，`自动性`确实能带来帮助，但在一个学习阶段过度重复学习应当谨慎。研究表明，这可能浪费宝贵的学习时间**。*现实是，一旦你在一个阶段掌握了基本概念，**在同一个阶段继续反复琢磨并不会增强你所希望的长期记忆连接**。更糟的是，过于集中于一种技巧就像只用锤子学习木工。一段时间后，你会觉得通过猛砸就能修复任何东西。*

**Using a subsequent study session to repeat what you're trying to learn is just fine and often valuable. It can strengthen and deepen your chunked neuron patterns.** But be wary; repeating something you already know perfectly well, is, face it, easy. *It can also bring the `illusion of competence` that you've mastered the full range of material, when you've actually only mastered the easy stuff.* Instead, you want to balance your studies by deliberately focusing on what you find more difficult. *This focusing on the more difficult material is called `deliberate practice`. It's often what makes the difference between a good student and a great student*.

**使用后续的学习阶段来重复你正在学习的内容是完全可以的，而且通常很有价值。这可以增强和加深你的信息块神经模式。**但要小心；重复你已经非常熟悉的内容，其实是很容易的。*这也可能带来`知识错觉`，而实际上你只掌握了简单的部分*。相反，你需要通过有意识地关注更困难的内容来平衡你的学习。*这种关注更困难材料的方式被称为`刻意练习`。这往往是优秀学生和优秀学生之间的区别*。

All this is also related to a concept known as `Einstellung`. In this phenomenon, **your initial simple thought, an idea you already have in mind or a neural pattern you've already developed and strengthened, may prevent a better idea or solution from being found.** We saw this in the focus pinball picture, where your initial pinball of thought went to the upper part of the brain, but the solution thought pattern was in the lower part. The crowded bumpers of the focus mode and the previous patterns you built can create a sort of rut that prevents you from springing to a new place where the solution might be found. Incidentally, the German word Einstellung means mindset. Basically you can remember einstellung as installing a roadblock because of the way you were initially looking at something. This kind of wrong approach is especially easy to do in sports and science, not to mention other disciplines, **because sometimes your initial intuition about what's happening or what you need to be doing is misleading. You have to unlearn your erroneous older ideas or approaches even while you're learning new ones.**

这一切还与一个叫做“`思维定势`”（Einstellung）的概念相关。在这一现象中，**你最初的简单想法、你已经掌握的概念或神经模式可能会阻碍更好的想法或解决方案的产生**。我们在聚焦弹球的图示中看到，初始的思维弹球上升到大脑的上部，而解决方案的思维模式则在下部。聚焦模式的拥挤和你之前建立的模式可能会造成一种障碍，阻止你跳到一个可能找到解决方案的新地方。顺便提一下，德语中的“Einstellung”意为心态。你可以把“`思维定势`”记作因为最初的视角而设置的路障。这种错误的方法在体育和科学领域尤其容易发生，更不用说其他学科，**因为有时你对正在发生的事情或需要做的事情的初步直觉可能是误导性的。你必须在学习新知识的同时，忘掉错误的旧观念或方法**。

**One significant mistake students sometimes make in learning is jumping into the water before they learn to swim.** In other words, they blindly start working on homework without reading the textbook, attending lectures, viewing online lessons, or even speaking with someone knowledgeable. This is a recipe for sinking. It's like randomly allowing a thought to, kind of pop off in the focus mode pinball machine, without paying any real attention to where the solution truly lies. **Understanding how to obtain real solutions is important in learning and in life.** Mastering a new subject means learning not only the basic chunks, but also learning how to select and use different chunks. **The best way to learn that is by practicing jumping back and forth between problems or situations that require different techniques or strategies**. This is called `interleaving`.

学生在学习中**有一个显著的错误就是在学会游泳之前就跳入水中**。换句话说，他们盲目开始做作业，而不去阅读教科书、参加讲座、观看在线课程，甚至与知识渊博的人交谈。这让学习“翻车”。就像在聚焦模式的弹球机中，随机让一个想法冒出来，而没有真正关注解决方案到底在哪里。**了解如何获得真正的解决方案在学习和生活中都很重要。**掌握一门新学科意味着不仅要学习基本的信息块，还要学习如何选择和使用不同的信息块。学习这一点的最佳方法是**练习在需要不同技巧或策略的问题或情境之间来回跳转**。这被称为`交错学习（interleaving）`。

**Once you have the basic idea of the technique down during your study session**, sort of like learning to ride a bike with training wheels, **start `interleaving` your practice with problems of different types or different types of approaches, concepts, procedures**. Sometimes this can be a little tough to do. A given section in a book, for example, is often devoted to a specific technique, so when you flip to that section you already know which technique you're going to be using. Still, do what you can to mix up your learning. *In science and math in particular it can help to look ahead at the more varied problem sets that are sometimes found at the end of chapters. Or you can deliberately try to make yourself occasionally pick out why some problems call for one technique as opposed to another.* **You want your brain to become used to the idea that just knowing how to use a particular concept, approach, or problemsolving technique isn't enough. You also need to know when to use it.**

**一旦在学习过程中掌握了某种技巧的基本概念**，就像带辅助轮学骑自行车一样，可以**开始将练习与不同类型的问题、方法、概念或步骤混合起来**。这种`交错学习`（interleaving）有助于**深化理解和灵活应用**，但有时可能会有些困难。例如，书中的某一章节通常专注于特定技巧，当翻到该章节时，你已经知道将使用哪种技巧。然而，尽量通过不同方式混合你的学习内容。尤其是在科学和数学领域，这种方法非常有效。*可以尝试提前查看章节末尾的综合问题集，这些问题往往更加多样化。此外，也可以有意识地分析并找出为什么某些问题适合使用一种特定技巧，而不是另一种。***目标是让大脑习惯于不仅仅知道如何使用某种概念、方法或解题技巧，还要知道何时以及为什么使用它。**

`Interleaving` your studies, making it a point to review for a test, for example, by skipping around through problems in the different chapters and materials can sometimes seem to make your learning a little more difficult, but in reality, it helps you learn more deeply. **Interleaving is extraordinarily important. Although practice and repetition is important in helping build solid neural patterns to draw on, it's interleaving that starts building `flexibility` and `creativity`.** *It's where you leave the world of practice and repetition, and begin thinking more independently. When you interleave within one subject or one discipline, you begin to develop your creative power within that discipline. When you interleave between several subjects or disciplines, you can more e asily make interesting new connections between chunks in the different fields, which can enhance your creativity even further.*

`交错学习`，即例如通过在不同章节和材料中跳过问题来复习以备考试，*有时可能让你的学习看起来更困难*，但实际上，它帮助你更深入地学习。**交错学习是极其重要的。虽然练习和重复在帮助建立坚实的神经模式以供调用方面很重要，但正是交错学习开始建立`灵活性`和`创造力`**。*它是你离开练习和重复的世界，开始更独立思考的地方。当你在一个学科或领域内进行交错学习时，你开始在该领域内发展你的创造力。当你在多个学科之间交错学习时，你可以更容易地在不同领域的信息块之间建立有趣的新连接，这可以进一步增强你的创造力。*

Of course it takes time to develop solid chunks of knowledge in different fields, so sometimes there's a **tradeoff**. *Developing expertise in several fields means you can bring very new ideas from one field to the other, but it can also mean that your expertise in one field or the other isn't quite as deep as that of the person who specializes in only one discipline*. On the other hand, **if you develop expertise in only one discipline, you may know it very deeply but you may become more deeply entrenched in your familiar way of thinking and not be able to handle new ideas**. *Philosopher of science Thomas Kuhn discovered that most paradigm shifts in science are brought about either young people or people who were originally trained in a different discipline. **They're not so easily trapped by einstellung, blocked thoughts due to their preceding training**. And of course there's the old saying that science progresses one funeral at a time as people entrenched in the old ways of looking at things die off.*

当然，在不同领域发展扎实的信息块需要时间，因此有时会有**取舍**。*在多个领域发展专业知识意味着你可以将一个领域的新观念带到另一个领域，但这也可能意味着你在某个领域的专业知识不如专注于一个学科的人那么深*。另一方面，**如果你只在一个学科中发展专业知识，虽然你可能了解得非常深入，但你可能会在熟悉的思维方式中更深地陷入，而无法处理新观念**。*科学哲学家托马斯·库恩发现，大多数科学范式转变要么是由年轻人推动，要么是那些最初在不同学科接受过训练的人推动。**他们不那么容易被“设定”束缚，即由于先前的训练而产生的思维障碍**。当然，还有一句老话：科学进步是一场葬礼接着一场葬礼，因为执迷于旧观念的人慢慢去世。*

Finally, don't make the mistake of thinking that learning only occurs in the kinds of subjects you acquire from teachers or books. When you teach a child how to deal effectively with a bully, or you fix a leaky faucet, or you quickly pack a small suitcase for a business trip to Hong Kong, all of these illustrate the outcomes of important aspects of learning. Physicist Richard Feynman was inspired in his Nobel Prize-winning work by watching someone throw a dinner plate into the air in a cafeteria. Mike Rowe of the television shows Dirty Jobs and Somebody's Gotta Do It shows how important and exciting learning can be in a variety of different, non-academic disciplines. I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning about learning.

最后，**不要误认为学习仅发生在你从老师或书本中获取的学科中**。*当你教孩子如何有效对付霸凌者，或修理漏水的水龙头，或快速为香港的商务旅行打包小行李时，这些都展示了学习的重要方面和成果。物理学家理查德·费曼在获得诺贝尔奖的工作中受到了在自助餐厅看到有人把晚餐盘扔向空中的启发*。电视节目《肮脏工作》和《总得有人做》中的迈克·罗所展示的多种不同非学术学科中的学习，体现了学习的重要性和激动人心的性质。我是芭芭拉·奥克利，感谢你关注学习的艺术。

### 2.9 Summary Week 2

In this video, I'm going to synthesize some of the main ideas of this week's videos. In other words, we'll chunk our week on chunking. Here we go:

在这个视频中，我将总结本周视频的主要思想。换句话说，我们将对本周关于信息块的内容进行概括。下面开始：

Chunks are pieces of information, neuroscientifically speaking, that are bound together through use and often through meaning. You can think of a chunk as a scintillating network of neurons that compactly synthesizes key ideas or actions.

**信息块是神经科学上讲的那些通过使用和意义紧密结合在一起的信息片段**。你可以将信息块视为一个闪烁的神经网络，它紧凑地综合了关键概念或行动。

Chunks can get bigger and more complex. But at the same time, they're a single **easy to access** item that you can fit like a ribbon into the slot on your working memory.

信息块可以变得更大更复杂，但同时它们又是一个**易于访问**的单一项，可以像丝带一样放入你的工作记忆中。

*Chunks are best built with focused, undivided attention, understanding of the **basic idea**, and **practice** to help deepen your patterns and to help you gain **big picture** context.*

*构建信息块的最佳方式是专注于、全神贯注于**基本概念**，并通过**实践**加深你的模式，以帮助你获得**全局视角**。*

`Simple recall`, trying to remember the key points without looking at the page, is one of the best ways to help the chunking process along. It seems to help build neural hooks. They help you better understand the material. Also try recalling material in places that are different from where you originally learned the material, so it becomes more deeply ingrained and accessible, regardless of what room you're in. This can be very helpful for tests.

`简单的回忆`，即试图在不看页面的情况下记住关键点，是促进信息块化过程的最佳方法之一。这似乎有助于建立神经钩子，帮助你更好地理解材料。此外，尝试在不同于最初学习材料的地方回忆内容，这样可以使信息更深入地扎根并更易于获取，无论你身处哪个房间。这对于考试非常有帮助。

`Transfer` is the idea that a chunk you've mastered in one area can often help you much more easily learn chunks of information in different areas that can share surprising commonalities.

`迁移`是指你在一个领域掌握的信息块，往往可以帮助你更轻松地学习不同领域中可能有惊人共性的知识块。

`Interleave` your learning by practicing your choice of different concepts, approach, and techniques all in one session. Chunks are very important, but they don't necessarily build flexibility, which is also important in becoming an expert with the material you're learning.

通过在一个学习会话中练习不同的概念、方法和技术来`交错学习`。信息块非常重要，但它们不一定能建立灵活性，而灵活性在成为所学材料的专家中同样重要。

`Illusions of competence` in learning – learn to recognize when you're fooling yourself about whether you're actually learning the material. Test yourself frequently. Using little minitests to see whether you're actually learning the material, or whether you've been fooling yourself, thinking you're learning when you're actually not. Recall is actually a form of minitesting. Try to avoid depending too much on highlighting, which can fool you into thinking that the material is going into your brain when it actually isn't.

在学习中产生的`能力错觉`——学会识别何时在自欺欺人，认为自己实际上在学习材料。要经常测试自己。使用小型测试来检查自己是否真的在学习材料，还是在自欺欺人，认为自己在学习但实际上并没有。回忆实际上是一种小测试。尽量避免过于依赖标记，这可能会让你误以为材料正在进入你的大脑，而实际上并不是。

**Mistakes are a good thing to make when you're learning. They allow you to catch illusions of competence**. Avoid practicing only the easy stuff, which can bring the illusion that you've mastered the material.

**错误在学习中是好事。它们让你发现能力错觉**。避免只练习简单的内容，这会让你产生掌握材料的错觉。

`Deliberately practice` what you find more difficult to gain full mastery of the material.

`刻意练习`你觉得更难的内容，以全面掌握材料。

`Einstellung` is when your initial thought, an idea you've already had in mind, or a neural pattern you've already developed well and strengthened, prevents a better idea or solution from being found. Or keeps you from being flexible enough to accept new, better, or more appropriate solutions.

`刻板观念`是指你最初的想法、已经在脑海中存在的观念，或者已经发展并强化的神经模式，阻止你发现更好的想法或解决方案，或者让你无法灵活地接受新的、更好的或更合适的解决方案。

`The Law of Serendipity` is helpful – **Lady Luck favors the one who tries**. Just pick one tiny thing out to learn, then another. Just keep trying and you'll be pleasantly surprised at the results. I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning about learning.

`机缘法则`很有帮助——**幸运女神偏爱那些尝试的人**。只需选择一个小的学习内容，然后再选一个。继续尝试，你会对结果感到惊喜。我是Barbara Oakley。感谢你学习关于学习的内容。

### 2.10 Interview with Dr. Norman Fortenberry - Learning at MIT

### 2.11 Interview with Scott Young, a "Marco Polo" of Learning

### 2.12 Interview with author, Amy Alkon

### 3.1 Introduction to Procrastination and Memory

This week we're going to be talking about two seemingly different ideas. `Procrastination` and `memory`. But the two topics are intimately related. Why? Because building solid chunks of long term memory, chunks that are easily accessible by your short term memory, takes time. It's not the kind of thing you want to be putting off until the last minute. You already have one good tool for procrastination. `The pomodoro`. That powerful 25 minute concentrated period of energized focus. This week we're going to fill you in with more information about how procrastination happens and simple ways to tackle it. And this is key. Ways that don't take much will power, then we'll move on to talking about some of the best ways to access your brain's most powerful long-term memory systems. I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning how to learn.

本周我们将讨论两个看似不同的主题：`拖延症`和`记忆`。但这两个主题是密切相关的。为什么呢？因为构建牢固的长期记忆块——这些记忆块可以通过你的短期记忆轻松访问——需要时间。这不是你想要留到最后一刻去做的事情。你已经有了一个有效的对抗拖延的工具：`番茄工作法`。这个强大的25分钟集中精力的工作时间段。这个星期，我们将为你提供更多关于拖延如何发生以及简单应对方法的信息。而这些方法是关键——不需要太多意志力。然后我们将讨论一些访问你大脑最强大长期记忆系统的最佳方法。我是Barbara Oakley。感谢你学习如何学习。

### 3.2 Tackling Procrastination: It’s easier and more valuable than you think

Arsenic is incredibly toxic. For centuries until modern methods of detection were discovered, murderers found it to be a very popular substance. So you can imagine the shock at the 48th meeting of the German Association of Arts and Sciences in 1875 when two men sat in the front of the audience and downed more than double a deadly dose of arsenic. The next day the men were back at the conference smiling and healthy. How is it possible to take something so bad for you and stay alive, and even look healthy, despite the damage being done at a microscopic level to your body? The answer has an uncanny relationship to procrastination that's what we're going to be talking about in the next few videos. 

砷是极其有毒的。几个世纪以来，直到现代检测方法被发明，谋杀者们发现它是一种非常受欢迎的物质。因此，想象一下在1875年德国艺术与科学协会第48次会议上，当两位男士在观众面前喝下超过致命剂量两倍的砷时，人们的震惊。第二天，这两位男士满脸笑容，身体健康。这到底是怎么可能的呢？摄入如此有害的物质，竟然还能活着，甚至看起来很健康，尽管你的身体在微观层面上正在受到损害。答案与拖延有着惊人的关联，这正是我们接下来几段视频要讨论的内容。

You've already learned one handy tool to help you with procrastination, the Pomodoro, that 25 minute period of uninterrupted focus followed by a bit of relaxation. This week we're going to learn more. Understanding a little about the cognitive psychology of procrastination, just like understanding the chemistry of poison, can help us develop healthy preventatives. In these videos, I'm going to teach you `the lazy person's approach to tackling procrastination`. **This means you'll be learning about your inner zombies – the routine, habitual responses your brain falls into as a result of specific cues. These `zombie responses` are often focused on making the here and now better. As you'll see you can trick some of these zombies into helping you fend off procrastination when you need to.** Not all procrastination is bad. Even if you're pretty good already in handling procrastination you'll learn some helpful insights here that can allow you to better prioritize your learning.

你已经学会了一个有用的工具来帮助应对拖延——番茄工作法，即25分钟的集中专注时间，之后是一些放松。这个星期我们将学习更多关于拖延的认知心理学知识，就像了解毒药的化学成分一样，能够帮助我们制定健康的预防措施。在这些视频中，我将教你`懒人的拖延应对策略`，这意味着你**将学习内心的“僵尸”——大脑因特定提示而陷入的常规、习惯性反应。这些`僵尸反应`往往专注于让眼前的体验更好。正如你将看到的，你可以欺骗一些这些僵尸，帮助你抵御拖延。**当你需要时，拖延并不总是坏事。即使你已经很擅长处理拖延，这里你也将学到一些有用的见解，让你能够更好地优先安排学习。

The reason that learning to avoid procrastination is so important is that good learning is a bit by bit activity. You want to **avoid cramming which doesn't build solid neural structures**. By putting the same amount of time into your learning but **spacing that learning out by starting earlier you'll learn better**. First things first. Unlike procrastination which is easy to fall into, **willpower is hard to come by. It uses a lot of neural resources**. You shouldn't waste willpower on fending off procrastination except when absolutely necessary. Best of all as you'll see you don't need to.

学习避免拖延的重要性在于，良好的学习是一个逐步进行的过程。你想要**避免死记硬背，因为这并不能建立牢固的神经结构**。如果你**将相同的学习时间分散开来，提前开始学习，你会学得更好**。首先，与容易陷入拖延不同，**自制力是很难获得的，它需要大量的神经资源**。你不应该在抵御拖延时浪费自制力，除非绝对必要。最棒的是，正如你将看到的，你并不需要这样做。

If you'll remember **we procrastinate about things that make us a little bit uncomfortable**. You think about something you don't particularly like and the pain centers of your brain light up so you shift and narrow your focus of attention to something more enjoyable. This causes you to feel better. At least temporarily but sadly the long term effects of habitual avoidance can be nasty. When you put off your studies it can become even more painful to think about studying it. You can choke on tests because you haven't laid the firm neural foundations you need to feel comfortable with the material. Procrastination can be a single monumentally important keystone bad habit, a habit in other words that influences many important areas of your life. If you improve your abilities in this area many other positive changes will gradually begin to unfold.

如果你还记得，**我们拖延的是那些让我们感到不舒服的事情**。当你想到一些不太喜欢的事情时，大脑的痛苦中心会被激活，因此你会将注意力转向更愉快的事情。这样短期内你会感到好一些，但可悲的是，习惯性回避的长期效果可能很糟糕。当你推迟学习时，思考学习的过程会变得更加痛苦。你可能在考试中表现不佳，因为你没有打下扎实的神经基础，无法对材料感到舒适。拖延可能是一个非常重要的基石坏习惯，也就是说，它影响着你生活中的许多重要领域。如果你能在这一领域提高能力，许多积极的变化将会逐渐展开。

Procrastination shares features with addiction. It offers temporary excitement and relief from sometimes boring reality. It's easy to fool yourself for example into thinking that the best use of any given moment is. Surfing the web for information instead of actually reading the textbook or doing the assigned problems. You start to tell yourself stories. For example you might tell yourself that organic chemistry requires special reasoning, your weakness, so of course you're doing very poorly at it. You devise irrational excuses that sound superficially reasonable like if I study too far ahead of the test I'll forget the material. If you're troubled by procrastination you may even start telling yourself that procrastination is an innate unchangeable characteristic. After all if procrastination were easily fixable wouldn't you have fixed it by now?

**拖延与成瘾有一些相似之处。它提供了暂时的兴奋感和对有时无聊现实的解脱**。*你很容易自欺欺人，认为在任何时刻，冲浪网页获取信息比实际阅读教科书或完成作业更有价值。你开始**编造故事**。例如，你可能告诉自己，有机化学**需要特殊的推理能力**，而这是你的弱项，因此你当然做得很糟糕。你会想出听起来很合理的不理性借口，比如“如果我提前学习考试的内容，我会忘记”。如果你被拖延困扰，甚至可能开始告诉自己，拖延是一个与生俱来的、不可改变的特征。*毕竟，如果拖延是容易解决的问题，你早就解决了。

The higher you go in your studies however the more important it is to take control of procrastination. **Habits that worked in earlier years can turn around and bite you.** What I'll show you in these next few videos is how you can become the master of your habit. You should be making the decisions, not your well-meaning but unthinking zombies, your habits. As you'll see, the strategies for dealing with procrastination are simple. It's just that sometimes they aren't intuitively obvious.

然而，随着学习的深入，控制拖延变得愈发重要。**以前有效的习惯可能会反噬你。**在接下来的视频中，我将展示如何掌握你的习惯。你应该做出决策，而不是由那些善意但无意识的僵尸（习惯）来决定。正如你将看到的，处理拖延的策略其实很简单，只是有时并不直观。

So let's return to that story that began this video. The arsenic eaters started with tiny doses of arsenic. In tiny doses, arsenic doesn't seem harmful. You can even build up an immunity to its effects. This can allow you to take larger doses and look healthy, even as the poison is slowly increasing your risk of cancer and ravaging your organs. In a similar way procrastinators put off just that one little thing. They do it again and again gradually growing used to it. They can even look healthy but the long term effects? Not so good. I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning how to learn.

回到视频开始的那个故事，吃砷者开始时只摄入微量砷。在微量摄入的情况下，砷似乎并无害处。你甚至可以对其影响产生免疫力。这可能使你能够摄入更大剂量，依然看起来健康，尽管毒素却在慢慢增加你罹患癌症的风险，并破坏你的器官。类似地，拖延者会推迟那一件小事，一次又一次，逐渐习惯这种状态。他们甚至看起来很健康，但长期效果却不容乐观。我是Barbara Oakley，感谢你学习如何学习。

### 3.3 Zombies Everywhere

Just imagine backing out of a driveway for the first time ever in your life. *For some of you, that might seem like a pretty exciting proposition. The first time you might do this you would be in hyper alert. The deluge of information coming at you would make the job seem almost impossibly difficult. But, once you've chunked how to back up down your driveway, all you have to do is think, let's go. And, off you go.* **Your brain goes into this sort of zombie mode, where it is only semi aware of a few key factors, instead of being overwhelmed by all the data.** It's the same idea with riding a bicycle. At first, it's really hard, later it's easy. Neuro-scientifically speaking, chunking is related to habit. Habit is an energy saver for us. **It allows us to free our mind for other types of activities.**

想象一下，第一次从车道上倒车。对你们中的一些人来说，这可能是一个相当激动人心的提议。*第一次倒车时，你会非常警觉。接踵而来的信息量会让这件事显得几乎不可能完成。但一旦你掌握了如何倒车的技巧，你只需想着“走吧”，然后就出发了。***你的大脑进入了一种半意识的“僵尸模式”，只关注几个关键因素，而不再被所有数据淹没。**这种情况和骑自行车一样，最初很困难，后来就容易了。从神经科学的角度来看，分块（chunking）与习惯有关。**习惯为我们节省能量，使我们能够将注意力释放到其他活动上**。

*You go into this habitual zombie mode far more often than you might think.* **That's the point of habit, you don't have to think in a focused manner about what you’re doing while you're performing the habit.** It saves energy. Habits can be good and bad. They can be brief, like absently brushing back your hair. Or they can be long, for example when you take a walk, or watch television for a few hours after you get home from work. 

*你进入这种习惯性的“僵尸模式”的频率比你想象的要高*。**这就是习惯的意义：在执行习惯时，你无需集中思考**。习惯可以是好也可以是坏。它们可以很短，比如无意识地理顺头发；也可以很长，比如下班后散步或看几个小时的电视。

You can think of habits as having four parts. The first is the `cue`. This is the trigger that launches you into zombie mode. The cue may be something as simple as seeing the first item in your to do list. Time to start next week's homework. Or seeing a text message from a friend. Time to stop work. A cue by itself is, neither helpful nor harmful; it's the routine. What we do in reaction to that cue, that's what matters. Number two – the `routine`. This is your `zombie mode`. The routine habitual response your brain is used to falling into when it receives the cue. Zombie responses can be useful, harmless, or sometimes harmful. Number three, the `reward`. Every habit develops and continues because it rewards us. It gives us an immediate little feeling of pleasure. *Procrastination's an easy habit to develop because the reward, moving your mind's focus to something more pleasant, happens so quickly and easily.* But good habits can also be rewarded. Finding ways to reward good study habits is important for escaping procrastination. Number four – the `belief`. Habits have power because of your belief in them. For example, you might feel you'll never be able to change your habits of putting off your studies until late in the day. To change a habit, you'll need to change your underlying belief. I'm Barbara Oakley, thanks for learning how to learn.

你可以将习惯分为四个部分。第一是`提示`。这是启动你进入僵尸模式的触发器。提示可能是看到待办事项列表中的第一项，意味着是时候开始下周的作业；或是看到朋友发来的短信，意味着是时候停下工作。仅仅一个提示本身并没有好坏之分，关键在于反应。第二是`常规`。这是你的`僵尸模式`。习惯性的反应是你的大脑在接收到提示时习惯性地进入的状态。僵尸反应可以是有用的、无害的，或者有时是有害的。第三是`奖励`。每一个习惯的形成和持续都因为它给我们带来了奖励。它会带来一种立即的愉悦感。*拖延是一个容易形成的习惯，因为将注意力转移到更愉快的事情上会迅速而轻松地得到奖励。*但好的习惯同样可以得到奖励。找到奖励良好学习习惯的方法，对于摆脱拖延至关重要。第四是`信念`。习惯的力量来源于你对它们的信念。例如，你可能会觉得自己永远无法改变拖延学习的习惯。要改变一个习惯，你需要改变其背后的信念。我是Barbara Oakley，感谢你学习如何学习。


### 3.4 Surf’s Up: Process versus Product

One of the best ways of being effective in your learning is to use mental tools and tricks to inspire and motivate yourself. First, when it comes to learning in general, you should realize that it's perfectly normal to start with a few negative feelings about beginning a learning session. Even when it's a subject you ordinary like, it's how you handle those feelings that matters. Researchers have found that non-procrastinators put their negative thinking aside saying things to themselves like: quit wasting time and just get on with it, once you get going, you'll feel better about it.

提高学习效率的最佳方法之一是使用心理工具和技巧来激励自己。首先，关于学习，*您应该意识到在开始学习时产生一些负面情绪是完全正常的。即使是您通常喜欢的科目*，**处理这些情绪的方式才是关键**。研究发现，不拖延的人会**将负面思维搁置一旁**，告诉自己：**“别浪费时间，开始吧；一旦开始，你就会感觉好些。”**

If you find yourself avoiding certain tasks because they make you feel uncomfortable, you should know there's another helpful way to re-frame things. And that's **to learn to focus on process not product**. *Process means, the flow of time and the habits and actions associated with that flow of time. As in, I'm going to spend 20 minutes working. Product is an outcome, for example, a homework assignment that you need to finish.* 

如果您发现自己因为某些任务让自己感到不适而避免去做，您应该知道还有一种有帮助的方法可以重新框架，那就是**专注于过程而非结果**。*过程意味着时间的流动以及与时间流动相关的习惯和行为，例如：“我将花20分钟进行学习。”而结果则是一个具体的成果，比如您需要完成的作业。*

To prevent procrastination you want **to avoid concentrating on product**. Instead, your attention should be **on building processes**. **Processes relate to simple habits**, *habits that coincidentally allow you to do the unpleasant tasks that need to be done*. For example, let’s say you don't like doing homework in a particular class. So you put off working on the homework. It's only five questions you think. How hard could that be? Deep down, you realize that answering these five questions could be a very lengthy job. *It's easier to live in a fantasy world where the five questions, or the ten page report or whatever, can be done at the last minute.* 

为了防止拖延，您要**避免过于关注结果**。相反，您的**注意力应放在建立过程上**。**过程与简单的习惯相关**，*这些习惯恰好能帮助您完成那些需要做的不愉快的任务*。例如，假设您不喜欢在某一门课上做作业，于是您把作业推迟。您心里想：“这只是五个问题，难道很难吗？”但实际上，您深知回答这五个问题可能需要花费相当长的时间。*生活在幻想中更容易，以为这五个问题或十页的报告可以在最后时刻完成。*

Your challenge is to avoid focusing on the product, the answers to the questions. *The product is what triggers the pain that causes you to procrastinate*. Instead, you need to focus on the process or processes – the small chunks of time you need over days or even weeks to answer the questions or prepare for tests. Who cares whether you finish the homework or grasp the key concepts in any one session. The whole point instead, is that *you calmly put forth your best effort for a short period*. 

您的挑战是避免关注结果，即问题的答案。*结果会引发痛苦，从而导致拖延*。相反，您需要专注于过程——在几天甚至几周内回答问题或准备考试所需的小段时间。谁在乎您是否在某一节课上完成了作业或掌握了关键概念。关键在于，您*冷静地在短时间内尽最大努力*。

Now process. Notice how in this picture physicist and surfer Garret Lacy is focused on the moment. Not on the accomplishment of having surfed that wave. For you, *one of the easiest ways to focus on process is to focus on doing a Pomodoro*, a 25 minute timed work session, not on completing a task. The essential idea here is that **the zombie habitual part of your brain likes processes because it can march mindlessly along**. It's far easier to enlist the friendly zombie habit to help with a process, than to help with a product. 

注意在这张图片中，物理学家和冲浪者 Garret Lacy 专注于当下，而不是已经冲浪成功的成就。对您来说，*专注于过程的一个简单方法是进行番茄工作法*，即25分钟的定时学习，而不是完成某个任务。这里的核心思想是，**大脑的习惯性部分喜欢过程，因为它可以无意识地进行**。让友好的僵尸习惯帮助完成一个过程，要比帮助完成一个结果容易得多。

By focusing on process rather than product, you **allow yourself to back away from judging yourself**, am I getting closer to finishing? And instead you allow yourself to relax into the flow of the work. The key is **when a distraction arises, which it inevitably will, you want to train yourself to just let it flow by**. Of course, setting yourself up so that distractions are minimal is also a very good idea. Many students find that either a quiet space or noise canceling headphones if, if you can afford them, can be helpful when they're really trying to concentrate. 

通过专注于过程而非结果，您可以**避免自我评判**：“我离完成还有多远？”而是让自己放松，融入工作之中。关键是，**当分心出现时（这是不可避免的），您要训练自己让它自然流过**。当然，设置一个干扰最小的环境也是非常好的主意。许多学生发现，在真正需要集中注意力时，安静的空间或降噪耳机（如果可以负担得起的话）是很有帮助的。

I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning above learning.

我是Barbara Oakley，感谢您学习如何学习。


### 3.5 Harnessing Your Zombies to Help You

> [ˈhɑːrnɪs] v. 控制并利用；（把动物）拴在一起（或拴到某物上）；给（马）套上挽具；连接，串联 n. （马的）挽具，马具；系带，吊带；日常工作

In this video, we're going to get into the specifics of harnessing your zombie powers of habit to help you avoid procrastination while *minimizing your use of willpower*. You don't want to do a full scale change of old habits. You just want to override parts of them and develop a few new ones. The trick to overriding a habit is to look to change your reaction to a cue. The only place you need to apply willpower is to change your reaction to the cue. To understand that, it helps to go back through the four components of habit and re-analyze them from the perspective of procrastination. 

在这个视频中，我们将具体探讨如何利用习惯的“僵尸力量”来帮助您避免拖延，同时*尽量减少对意志力的使用*。您不需要彻底改变旧习惯，只需覆盖部分习惯并发展一些新习惯。覆盖习惯的关键是改变您对提示的反应。您只需在提示出现时运用意志力来改变反应。为了理解这一点，回顾习惯的四个组成部分，并从拖延的角度重新分析它们是很有帮助的。

The first one is `the cue`. Recognize what launches you into your zombie procrastination mode. **Cues usually fall into one of the four following categories: 1. Location 2. Time 3. How you feel, and 4. Reactions either to other people or to something that just happened.** Do you look something up on the web and then find yourself web surfing? Does a text message disturb your studying taking you 10 minutes to get back into the flow of things even when you try to keep yourself on task? *The issue with procrastination is that because it's an automatic habit, you're often unaware that you've begun to procrastinate. You can prevent the most damaging cues by shutting off your cell phone or keeping yourself away from the internet and other distractions for brief periods of time, as when you're doing a pomodoro.*

第一个是`提示`。识别是什么促使您进入拖延的“僵尸模式”。**提示通常分为以下四类：1. 地点 2. 时间 3. 您的情绪 4. 对他人或某事发生的反应。**您是否在网上查找某些东西后开始随意浏览网页？短信打扰了您的学习，让您花费十分钟才重新进入状态，即使您努力保持专注。*拖延的问题在于，由于它是自动习惯，您常常没有意识到自己已经开始拖延。您可以通过关掉手机或在进行番茄工作法时短时间远离互联网和其他干扰来防止最具破坏性的提示。*

Number two, `the routine`. Let's say then instead of doing your studies you often divert your attention to something less painful. Your brain wants to automatically go into this routine when you've gotten your cue. So **this, is the reaction clue where you must actively focus on re-wiring your old habit**. The key to re-wiring is to **have a plan**. **Developing a new ritual** can be helpful. *Some students make it a habit to leave their phone in their car when they head in for class which removes a potent distraction. Many students discovered the value of settling into a quiet spot in the library or closer to home, the productive effects of simply sitting in a favorite chair at the proper time with all Internet access disconnected*. **Your plan may not work perfectly at first, but just keep at it.** Adjust the plan if necessary, and savor those victories when your plan works. **Don't try to change everything at once**. The Pomodoro technique can be especially helpful in shifting your reaction to the cues.

第二，`例行公事`。假设您经常在学习时分散注意力，转而做一些更轻松的事情。您的大脑在得到提示时会自动进入这个例行公事。因此，这是您必须**主动集中精力重新连接旧习惯的反应提示**。重新连接的关键是**制定计划**。**建立新的仪式**可能会有所帮助。*有些学生养成将手机留在车里的习惯，这样就去除了强烈的干扰。许多学生发现，在图书馆或家附近的安静地方坐下，或者在适当的时间坐在自己喜欢的椅子上、断开互联网连接，可以产生很好的效果*。**您的计划可能一开始并不完美，但请继续努力**。如果有必要，请调整计划，并在计划有效时享受那些小胜利。**不要试图一次性改变所有事情**。番茄工作法在改变对提示的反应时尤其有效。

Number three is the `reward`. This can sometimes require a little bit of investigation. Why are you procrastinating? Can you substitute an emotional payoff, maybe a feeling of pride for accomplishing something, even if it's small, a sense of satisfaction? Can you win a small internal bet or a contest about something you've turned into a personal game or allow yourself to indulge in a latte or read a favorite web site, provide yourself, maybe, with an evening of mindless television or web surfing without guilt, and will you give yourself a bigger reward for a bigger achievement? Maybe movie tickets or a sweater or an utterly frivolous purchase. Remember that **habits are powerful because they create neurological cravings**. It helps to add a new reward if you want to overcome your previous cravings. Only once your brain starts expecting that reward will the important rewiring take place that will allow you to create new habits. Many people find that *setting a reward at a specific time, for example, breaking for lunch with a friend at the deli at noon or stopping the main task at 5 p.m., gives a solid, `mini deadline` that can help spur work*. Don't feel bad if you find you have trouble getting into a flow state at first. I sometimes find it takes a few days of drudgery, through a few cycles of the Pomodoro technique before flow begins to unfold. And I find myself starting to enjoy work on a new topic. Also remember that *the better you get at something, the more enjoyable it can become*. 

第三，`奖励`。这有时需要一些调查。您为什么会拖延？您是否可以替代情感上的满足感，比如完成某件事情带来的自豪感，即使它很小？您可以制定一个小的内部赌注或比赛，把某件事变成个人游戏，或者允许自己享受一杯拿铁，或阅读一个喜欢的网站，给自己一个无愧的晚上，随意看电视或上网，或者在取得更大成就时给予自己更大的奖励，比如电影票、一件毛衣或一个完全无用的购买。记住，**习惯之所以强大，是因为它们会产生神经渴望**。如果您想克服之前的渴望，增加新的奖励会有所帮助。只有当您的大脑开始期待该奖励时，重要的重新连接才会发生，使您能够创造新的习惯。许多人发现，*在特定时间设定奖励，比如中午与朋友在熟食店吃午餐，或下午5点停止主要任务，提供一个坚实的`小截止日期`，有助于激励工作*。如果您发现自己一开始很难进入状态，不要感到沮丧。我发现，有时需要几天的枯燥，经历几轮番茄工作法，才能开始享受新主题的工作。此外，记住，*您在某件事情上越熟练，它就变的越有趣。*

Number four is the `belief`. The most important part of **changing your procrastination habit is the belief that you can do it**. You may find that when the going gets stressful. You long to fall back into old, more comfortable habits. Belief that your new system works is what can get you through. Part of what can underpin is to develop a new community. Hang out with classmates, or virtually hang out with MOOC-mates, who may have that can-do philosophy that you too want to develop. Developing and encouraging culture with like-minded friends can help us remember the values that in moments of weakness we tend to forget. 

第四，`信念`。改变拖延习惯最重要的部分是**相信自己能够做到**。*当压力变大时，您可能会渴望回归旧的、更舒适的习惯。相信您的新系统有效能够让您坚持下去*。部分支撑这种信念的方式是建立一个新的社群。与同学或在线课程的同学一起交流，培养一种积极的思维哲学。与志同道合的朋友建立和鼓励文化，可以帮助我们在脆弱时刻记住我们常常忘记的价值观。

I'm Barbara Oakley, thanks for learning how to learn.

我是Barbara Oakley，感谢您学习如何学习。

### 3.6 Juggling Life and Learning

Learning for most people involves a `complex balancing` of many different tasks. A good way for you to keep perspective about what you're trying to learn and accomplish, is to *once a week write a brief weekly list of key tasks in a planner journal*. Then each day on another page of your planner journal, write a list of the tasks that you can reasonably work on or accomplish. Try to write this daily task list the evening before. Why the evening before? Research has shown that this helps your subconscious to grapple with the tasks on the list so you can figure out how to accomplish them. Writing the list before you go to sleep, enlists your zombies, to help you accomplish the items on the list the next day. 

对大多数人来说，学习涉及许多不同任务的`复杂平衡`。保持对您所学习和要完成的事情的清晰视角的好方法，是*每周在计划日记中写一个简短的关键任务列表*。然后每天在计划日记的另一页上，写下您可以合理完成的任务列表。*尽量在前一天晚上写下这个日常任务列表*。为什么选择前一天晚上？研究表明，这有助于您的潜意识处理列表上的任务，从而帮助您找到完成这些任务的方法。在睡觉前写下任务，可以调动您的“僵尸习惯”，帮助您在第二天完成列表上的项目。

*If you don't write your tasks down on a list, they lurk at the edge of the four or so slots of your working memory, **taking up valuable mental real estate***. But **once you make a task list, it frees working memory for problem solving**. So let's look at one of my daily task lists. As you can see here, there are only six items. Some are `process oriented`. For example, I have a paper due to a journal in several months. So, I spend a little focus time on most days working towards completing it. A few items are `product oriented`. But that is only because they are doable within a limited period of time. Note my reminders. I wanted to keep my focus on each item when I'm working on it. And I want to have fun. I did catch myself getting side tracked, because I forgot to shut down my email. To get myself back into gear, I set a 22 minute Pomodoro challenge, using a timer on my computer desktop. Why 22 minutes? Well, why not? I, I don't have to do the same thing each time. And notice, too, that by moving to Pomodoro mode, I've switched to a process orientation. 

*如果您不将任务写在列表上，它们就会在工作记忆的边缘徘徊，**占据宝贵的心理空间***。但一旦您**制作了任务列表，它就为解决问题释放了工作记忆**。让我们看看我的一个日常任务列表。如您所见，只有六项任务。其中一些是`过程导向`的。例如，我有一篇论文将在几个月后提交，所以我每天花一点时间朝着完成它的方向努力。还有一些是`产品导向`的，但那是因为它们在有限的时间内可以完成。请注意我的提醒，我希望在工作时专注于每一项任务，并且要有乐趣。我发现自己因为忘了关闭电子邮件而分心。为了重新进入状态，我设置了一个 22 分钟的番茄工作法挑战，使用计算机桌面的计时器。为什么是 22 分钟？因为我可以灵活选择时间。请注意，通过切换到番茄工作法，我已切换到过程导向。

None of the items on my list is **too big**, because I've got other things going on in my day. Meetings to go to, a lecture to give. Sometimes I sprinkle a few tasks that involve physical motion on my list, even if it's just cleaning something. Which, I'll admit, isn't ordinarily one of my favorite things to do. Somehow because I'm using them as diffuse mode breaks, I often look forward to them. Mixing other tasks up with your learning seems to make everything more enjoyable and keeps you from prolonged and unhealthy bouts of sitting. Over time, as I've gained more experience, I've gotten much better at gauging how long it takes to do any given task. You'll find yourself **improving quickly as you become more realistic about what you can reasonably do in any given time**. Make notes in your planner journal about what works and what doesn't. 

列表上的任务**没有一个过于宏大**，因为我还有其他事务要处理，例如会议和讲座。有时，我会在列表中添加一些涉及身体活动的任务，即使只是清理一些东西。老实说，这通常不是我喜欢做的事情。但由于我将它们作为放松的间歇，我常常期待这些任务。将其他任务与学习混合似乎使一切变得更加愉快，并能避免长时间的不健康坐姿。随着时间的推移，随着经验的增加，我对完成任何任务所需时间的判断变得更好。您会发现，**当您对自己在任何给定时间内合理可以完成的事情变得更加现实时，进步会很快**。在您的计划日记中记录哪些有效，哪些无效。

Notice my `goal finish time` for the day, 5 p.m. doesn't seem quite right, does it? But it is right, and it's one of the most important components of your daily planner journal. **Planning your quitting time is as important as planning your working time**. Generally, I aim to quit at 5 p.m., although when I'm learning something new, it can sometimes be a pleasure to look at it again after I've taken an evening break, just before I go to sleep, and occasionally, there's a major project that I'm wrapping up, like say, this MOOC that has me running into a bit of overtime. 

请注意，我的`目标结束时间`是下午5点，似乎并不完全对，但实际上是正确的，这是您日常计划日记中最重要的组成部分之一。**规划结束时间与规划工作时间同样重要**。通常，我的目标是下午 5 点结束工作，尽管当我学习新内容时，休息后在睡觉前再次查看这些内容，有时也是一种乐趣。偶尔，在我完成一项重要项目时，例如这个 MOOC，我会延长一点时间。

You might think, well, yeah, you know, but you're a professor who's shall we say, past your youthful study days. Of course an early quitting time is fine for you. However, one of my most admired study experts, Cal Newport, used the 5 p.m., quitting time through most of his student career. He ended up getting his PhD from MIT. In other words, this method, implausible though it may seem for some, can work for undergraduate and graduate students in rigorous academic programs.

您可能会想，当然，您是一位教授，已经过了年轻的学习时期。早早结束工作对您来说当然没问题。然而，我非常钦佩的一位学习专家 Cal Newport 在他的大部分学生生涯中也采用了下午5点的结束时间。他最终获得了 MIT 的博士学位。换句话说，这种方法，虽然对于一些人来说似乎不太可行，但对于本科生和研究生在严格的学术项目中是有效的。

Time after time, **those who are committed to maintaining healthy leisure time along with their hard work, outperform those who doggedly pursue an endless treadmill**. Of course, your life may not lend itself to such a schedule with breaks and leisure time. You may be running on fumes with two jobs and too many classes. But however your life is going, try to squeeze a little break time in. 

一次又一次，**那些致力于保持健康的休闲时间与辛勤工作相结合的人，往往比那些一味追求无尽工作的人表现更出色**。当然，您的生活可能并不适合这样有间歇和休闲时间的日程安排。您可能在两份工作和太多课程之间精疲力竭。但无论您的生活怎样，都尽量挤出一点休息时间。

One more thing – as writing coach Daphne Graygrant recommends to her writing clients, **eat your frogs first in the morning**. Try to work on a most important and most disliked task first. At least just one Pomodoro, as soon as you wake up. This is incredibly effective. Do you need to sometimes make changes in your plans because of unforeseen events? Of course, but remember the law of serendipity; Lady Luck favors the one who tries. Planning well is part of trying. Keep your eye on your learning goal, and try not to get too unsettled by occasional roadblocks. 

还有一点——正如写作教练 Daphne Graygrant 对她的写作客户所推荐的，**早上先处理最重要和最不喜欢的任务**。尽量在早上醒来后完成至少一个番茄工作法的时间。这是非常有效的。您是否有时需要因不可预见的事件而调整计划？当然，但请记住机缘法则；好运常青睐于那些努力尝试的人。良好的规划是尝试的一部分。始终关注您的学习目标，尽量不要被偶尔的障碍所打扰。

I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning how to learn.

我是Barbara Oakley，感谢您学习如何学习。

### 3.7 Summing Up Procrastination

**Learning well often involves bit by bit, day by day building of solid neural scaffolds**. Rather like a weight lifter builds muscle with day to day exercise. This is why tackling procrastination is so incredibly important. You want to keep up with your learning and avoid last minute cramming. So with that, here's an overview of the key aspects of tackling procrastination.
  - Keep a planner journal so you can easily track when you reach your goals and observe what does and doesn't work. 
  - Commit yourself to certain routines and tasks each day. Write your planned tasks out the night before so your brain has time to dwell on your goals and help ensure success. 
  - Arrange your work into a series of small challenges. Always make sure you, and your zombies, get lots of rewards. 
  - Take a few minutes to savor the feelings of happiness and triumph, which also gives your brain a chance to temporarily change modes. 
  - Deliberately delay rewards until you've finished a task. Watch for procrastination cues. 
  - Try putting yourself in new surroundings with few procrastination cues, such as the quiet section of a library. 
  - Gain trust in your new system. You want to work hard during times of focused concentration and also to trust your system enough so that when it comes time to relax, you actually relax without feelings of guilt or worry. 
  - Have back up plans for when you still procrastinate. No one’s perfect after all. 
  - Eat your frogs first every day. Happy experimenting.

**有效学习通常需要一点一滴地逐步构建坚实的神经框架**，就像举重者通过日常锻炼来锻炼肌肉一样。这就是为什么应对拖延症如此重要的原因。您想跟上学习进度，避免临时抱佛脚。那么，以下是应对拖延症的关键要素概述：
  - 保持计划日志：方便**跟踪目标达成情况，观察什么有效，什么无效**。
  - **每天承诺某些例行任务**：计划任务时，晚上提前写下，以便大脑有时间思考目标，确保成功。
  - 将工作分解成小挑战：这样可以更容易地应对和完成。
  - 确保奖励自己和“僵尸”：花几分钟享受成功带来的快乐，这也给大脑一个暂时改变模式的机会。
  - 在完成任务后再奖励自己：故意延迟奖励，直到任务完成。
  - 注意拖延的信号：尝试将自己置于新的环境中，比如图书馆的安静区域，减少拖延的诱因。
  - 信任您的新系统：在集中注意力时努力工作，并信任系统，以便放松时真正放松，而不感到内疚或担忧。
  - 制定备选计划：即使在拖延时也要有后备计划。毕竟，没有人是完美的。
  - 每天先处理最困难的任务：先“吃掉青蛙”。

### 3.8 Diving Deeper into Memory

In this video and the next, we're going to deepen our understanding of memory. As you're probably beginning to understand, memory is only part of learning and developing expertise but it's often an important part. It may surprise you to learn that we have outstanding **visual and spatial memory systems** that can help form part of our long-term memory. Here's what I mean. If you were asked to look around a house you never visited before, you'd soon have a sense of the general furniture layout and where the rooms were, color scheme, the pharmaceuticals in the bathroom cupboard. In just a few minutes, your mind would acquire and retain thousands of new pieces of information. Even weeks later, you'd still hold far more in your mind than if you'd spent the same amount of time staring at a blank wall. *Your mind is built to **retain** this kind of **general information** about a place.* You can greatly enhance your ability to remember if you tap into these naturally super-sized, visual, spatial memorization abilities. *Our ancestors never needed a vast memory for names or numbers but they did need a memory for how to get back home from the three day deer hunt, or for the location of those plump blueberries on the rocky slopes to the South of the camp. These evolutionary needs helped lock in a superior "where things are" and "how they look" memory system.*

在这个视频和下一个视频中，我们将加深对记忆的理解。正如你可能已经开始明白的那样，记忆只是学习和发展专业技能的一部分，但它通常是一个重要的部分。你可能会惊讶地发现，我们有着**出色的视觉和空间记忆系统**，可以帮助构建我们长期记忆的一部分。让我来解释一下。*如果你被要求看一看一栋你从未到过的房子，你很快就会对整体家具布局、房间的位置、色调、浴室柜里的药品有一个大致的了解。*在短短几分钟内，你的大脑会获取并保持成千上万条新信息。即使是几周后，你仍然会记住比你花同样时间盯着一面空白墙更多的信息。你的*大脑天生就是用来**保持**关于一个地方的这种**总体信息**的*。如果你能利用这些天生超强的视觉和空间记忆能力，你的记忆力将会大大增强。*我们的祖先从来不需要记住大量的名字或数字，但他们确实需要记住如何从三天的鹿猎中找到回家的路，或者记住那些位于营地南方岩坡上的饱满蓝莓的位置。这些进化上的需求帮助他们建立了一种优越的“事物在哪里”和“它们长什么样”的记忆系统。*

To begin tapping into your visual memory system try making a very memorable visual image representing one key item you want to remember. For example, here's an image you could use to remember Newton's second law, f = ma. This is a fundamental relationship relating force to mass and acceleration (force = mass x acceleration). And it only took humans, oh, a couple of hundred thousand years to figure out. The letter f in the formula could stand for flying, m could stand for mule, and a, well that's up to you. Part of the reason an image is so important to memory is that images connect directly to your right brain's visual spatial centers. The image helps you encapsulate a seemingly humdrum and hard to remember concept by tapping into visual areas with enhanced memory abilities. The more neural hooks you can build by evoking the senses, the easier it will be for you to recall the concept and what it means. Beyond merely seeing the mule, you can smell the mule, you can feel the same windy pressure the mule is feeling.. You can even, hear the wind whistling past. The funnier and more evocative the images, the better. 

为了开始利用你的视觉记忆系统，试着为你想记住的一个关键项目创造一个非常难忘的视觉图像。例如，这里有一个你可以用来记住牛顿第二定律（f = ma）的图像。这是一个基本的关系，描述了力与质量和加速度之间的关系（力 = 质量 × 加速度）。而且，人类花了，嗯，大约几十万年的时间才弄明白这个公式。公式中的字母f可以代表飞翔，m可以代表骡子，而a，嗯，那就由你来决定了。*图像之所以对记忆如此重要，部分原因是因为图像直接与右脑的视觉空间中心连接。*这个图像帮助你通过调动具有增强记忆能力的视觉区域，将一个看似平淡且难以记住的概念呈现出来。你可以通过唤起感官来建立更多的神经连接，这样你就更容易回忆起这个概念及其含义。除了看到那只骡子，你还可以闻到骡子的气味，你可以感觉到骡子正在感受到的风压。你甚至可以听到风呼啸而过。图像越有趣、越引人入胜，它对记忆的帮助就越大。

Focusing your attention brings something into your temporary working memory, but for that something to move from working memory to long term memory two things should happen: the idea should be memorable; there's a gigantic flying mule braying f = ma on my couch, and it must be repeated. Otherwise remember your tiny metabolic vampires, they can suck away the neural pattern related to that memory before it can strengthen and solidify. Repetition is important. Even when you make something memorable, repetition helps get that memorable item firmly lodged into long-term memory. Remember to repeat not a bunch of times in one day but sporadically over several days. Index cards can often be helpful. Writing and saying what you're trying to learn seems to enhance retention. For example, if you're trying to learn concepts in physics you might take an index card and write the greek letter rho (ρ). That's a common abbreviation for density. You'd write it on one side and you'd write the remaining information on the other. Handwriting helps you to more deeply encode, that is convert into neural memory structures what you are trying to learn. While you're writing out the kilograms per cubic meter (kilogram/m3) you might imagine a shadowy kilogram just feel that mass lurking in an oversize piece of baggage that happens to be one meter on each side. The more you can turn what you're trying to remember into something memorable, the easier it will be to recall. You'll want to say the word and its meaning aloud to start setting auditory hooks to the material. Next, just look at the side of the card with the Greek letter rho (ρ) on it, and see whether you can remember what's on the other side of the card. If you can't, flip it over and remind yourself what you're supposed to know. If you can remember, put the card away. Now, do something else. Perhaps prepare another card and test yourself on it. Once you have several cards together, try running through them all and even mixing them around to see if you can remember them. This helps interleave your learning. Don't be surprised if you struggle a bit. Once you've given your cards a good try, put them away. Wait and take them out again, maybe before you go to sleep. Remember that sleep is when your mind repeats patterns and pieces together solutions. Briefly repeat what you want to remember over several days. Perhaps for a few minutes each morning or each evening. Gradually extend the time between the repetitions as the material firms itself into your mind. By increasing your spacing as you become more certain of mastery, you'll lock the material more firmly into place. Great flash card systems like Anki have built in algorithms that repeat in scale ranging from days to months. Interestingly, one of the best ways to remember people's names, is to simply try to retrieve the people's names from memory at increasing time intervals, after first learning the name.

集中注意力将某个事物带入你的短期工作记忆中，但要让这个事物从工作记忆转移到长期记忆中，必须发生两件事：这个想法应该是值得记住的；比如说我沙发上的巨大飞骡子正在大声叫喊着 f = ma，而且它必须重复。否则，记住那些小小的代谢吸血鬼，它们可能会在神经模式尚未强化和巩固之前，将与该记忆相关的模式吸走。重复是非常重要的。即使你已经让某个事物变得值得记住，重复有助于将这个可记住的项目牢牢地固守在长期记忆中。记住，不是一天之内重复很多次，而是要在几天内间歇性地重复。索引卡通常很有帮助。写出并朗读你想学习的内容似乎有助于增强记忆力。例如，如果你在学习物理概念，你可以拿一张索引卡，写下希腊字母 rho (ρ)，它是密度的常见缩写。你可以在一面写下它，另一面写下相关的内容。手写有助于你更深刻地编码，即将你想要学习的内容转化为神经记忆结构。当你写下每立方米千克（千克/立方米）时，你可以想象一个阴影般的千克，感觉到它像一个超大的行李箱一样的质量，而这个行李箱正好是每边一米。你越能把你想记住的东西转化为值得记住的内容，你就越容易回忆起来。你可以大声说出这个词和它的含义，开始为它的听觉记忆建立钩子。接下来，只看卡片上写有希腊字母 rho (ρ) 的一面，看看你是否能记住另一面上的内容。如果记不住，翻过来提醒自己应该记住什么。如果记得住，就把卡片收起来。然后，做些其他事情。也许准备另一张卡片并测试自己。一旦你有了几张卡片，试着把它们全部浏览一遍，甚至把它们混合起来，看看你是否能记住它们。这有助于将你的学习交替进行。不要对自己有所挣扎感到惊讶。一旦你对卡片做了充分的测试，就把它们放起来。等待几天，再拿出来，也许是在睡觉前。记住，睡眠是你大脑重复模式并拼接解决方案的时候。简短地重复你想记住的内容，分散在几天内。也许每天早上或每晚花几分钟。随着材料逐渐巩固到你的大脑中，逐渐延长重复之间的时间。通过在你更加确信掌握了材料后增加重复间隔，你将能更牢固地把它们锁定。像 Anki 这样的优秀闪卡系统，内置了从几天到几个月的重复算法。有趣的是，记住人名的最佳方法之一，就是在学习名字之后，尝试在逐渐增加的时间间隔中从记忆中提取人名。

### 3.9 What is Long Term Memory?

Welcome back to Learning How to Learn. What would it be like if you couldn't learn new things, you would not be able to remember new people you met, or remember what you were told? This actually happened to a famous patient in the annals of memory research whose initials were HM. At the age of 27, HM had an operation for epilepsy that took out his hippocampus on both sides of his brain. The hippocampus has a shape of a seahorse and is named from the Greek hippos, meaning horse and kampos, meaning sea monster. The operation was a success. The epilepsy was cured but the price was steep. HM could no longer remember new things. He had become profoundly amnesic. Curiously, you could have a normal conversation with HM, but if you left the room for a few minutes, he could not remember you or what you had discussed. In the film Memento, the character played by Guy Pearce had this form of amnesia from a concussion. Note that he tattooed his body with messages, so that he would not forget what he had to do. HM could learn other things, like a new motor skill, but he could not remember having learned it. There are multiple memory systems for different types of learning. From the studying HM and animals with similar operations, we have learned that the hippocampus is important part of a brain system for learning and memory of facts and events. Without the hippocampus and its inputs, it is not possible to store new memories in the cortex, a process called memory consolidation that can take many years. HM could remember things from his childhood but he had trouble remembering things that had occurred in the years just before his operation, things that had not yet become fully consolidated. Something similar happens when you have a bad concussion but this usually resolves, unlike HM who never improved. Memories are not fixed but living, breathing parts of your brain that are changing all of the time. Whenever you recall a memory, it changes, a process called, reconsolidation. It is even possible to implant false memories, which are indistinguishable from real ones by simply suggesting and imagining, especially in children who have vivid imaginations. Here is a summary: The green process of consolidation takes the brain state in active memory and stores it in long term memory by modifying synapses on the dendrites of neurons. These long term memories can remain dormant for a long time until the memory is retrieved and reinstated, by the red process, in short term working memory. The reinstated memory is in a new context, which can itself be transferred to long term memory, thereby, altering the old memory through reconsolidation. Our memories are intertwined with each other. As we learn new things, our old memories also change. Like consolidation, reconsolidation also occurs during sleep. This is why it is more effective to space learning over time, rather than mass learning all at once. If you want to study something for an hour, you will retain it longer if you spend 10 minutes each month over a semester than an hour on one day. In contrast, if you wait until the day before an exam to cram the material, you may be able to retrieve for the next day on the exam but it will quickly fade from memory.

欢迎回来学习如何学习。如果你无法学习新事物，无法记住新认识的人或听到的内容，会是什么样的情形呢？这在记忆研究的历史上发生在一位著名患者身上，他的名字是HM。27岁时，HM因癫痫接受了手术，切除了他大脑两侧的海马体。海马体形状像海马，得名于希腊语中的“hippos”（马）和“kampos”（海怪）。手术成功，癫痫得以治愈，但代价却是沉重的。HM再也无法记住新事物，变得严重失忆。有趣的是，你可以与HM进行正常的对话，但如果你离开房间几分钟，他就无法记起你或你们讨论的内容。

在电影《记忆碎片》中，盖·皮尔斯饰演的角色也患有类似的失忆症，他通过在身上刺字来提醒自己必须做的事情。HM能够学习新的运动技能，但他无法记得自己曾经学过这些技能。不同类型的学习对应着大脑的多种记忆系统。通过研究HM和类似的动物，我们了解到海马体是学习和记忆事实和事件的重要部分。没有海马体及其输入，无法将新记忆存储在皮层中，这个过程称为记忆巩固，可能需要多年时间。

HM能够记住童年时期的事情，但对手术前不久发生的事件则难以记忆，因为这些事件尚未完全巩固。类似的情况也会在严重脑震荡后发生，但通常会恢复，而HM则永远没有改善。记忆不是固定的，而是大脑中不断变化的活生生的部分。每当你回忆起某个记忆时，它都会发生变化，这个过程称为再巩固（reconsolidation）。甚至可以通过暗示和想象来植入虚假记忆，这些虚假记忆与真实记忆几乎无法区分，特别是在想象力丰富的儿童中。

总结一下：绿色过程的巩固将活跃的记忆状态转化为长期记忆，通过修改神经元树突上的突触来存储。这些长期记忆可能会在长时间内保持休眠状态，直到通过红色过程在短期工作记忆中被提取和恢复。恢复的记忆处于新的上下文中，这本身也可以转移到长期记忆中，从而通过再巩固改变旧记忆。我们的记忆彼此交织，随着新事物的学习，旧记忆也会发生变化。像巩固一样，再巩固也会在睡眠中进行。这就是为什么将学习分散在时间中比一次性集中学习更有效。如果你想学习一小时，分散到整个学期每月学习十分钟，记忆的保持时间更长；而如果你在考试前一天突击复习，虽然可能第二天能在考试中回忆起来，但很快就会淡忘。

In addition to neurons, brains have several types of supporting cells called glial cells. The astrocyte is the most abundant glial cell in the human brain. Astrocytes provide nutrients to neurons, maintain extra cellular ion balance, and are involved with repair following injury. In this photo of the cortex, the astrocytes are staying green and the neurons are blue. The intricate arms of the astrocytes wrap around the neurons, each embracing thousands of synapses. A recent experiment suggest that these astrocytes may also have an important role in learning. When human astrocytes were put into mouse brains, the humanized mice learned faster. Interestingly, when Einstein's brain was examined to find out what made him so awesomely creative, the only difference that could be found was that he had many more astrocytes than the average human. Could astrocytes be the key to understanding human intelligence? Well, the more we learn about the brain, the more may we have to rethink learning. I'm Terry Sejnowski. Happy learning, until we meet again.

除了神经元，大脑还有几种支持细胞，称为胶质细胞。星形胶质细胞是人脑中最丰富的胶质细胞。星形胶质细胞为神经元提供营养，维持细胞外离子平衡，并在受伤后参与修复。在这张皮层的照片中，星形胶质细胞呈绿色，神经元呈蓝色。星形胶质细胞的复杂分支包裹着神经元，每个星形胶质细胞拥抱着成千上万的突触。

最近的一项实验表明，这些星形胶质细胞在学习中可能也发挥着重要作用。当人类星形胶质细胞被放入小鼠的大脑中时，这些“人化”的小鼠学习速度更快。有趣的是，当科学家检查爱因斯坦的大脑时，发现他与普通人最大的不同就是他的星形胶质细胞数量远超常人。星形胶质细胞是否是理解人类智力的关键？随着我们对大脑了解的加深，我们可能需要重新思考学习的方式。我是特里·赛诺斯基，祝你学习愉快，期待下次再见。



### 3.10 Creating Meaningful Groups and the Memory Palace Technique


In this video, we're going to delve deeper into memory. Another key to memorization is to create meaningful groups that simplify the material. Let's say you wanted to remember four plants that help ward off vampires. Garlic, rose, hawthorn and mustard. The first letters abbreviate to GRHM, so all you need to do to remember is use the image of a graham cracker. It's much easier to remember numbers by associating them with memorable events. The year 1965 might be when one of your relatives was born for example. Or you can associate numbers with a numerical system you're familiar with. For example, 11.0 seconds is a good running time for the 100 meter dash. Or 75 might be the number of stitches on a knitting needle for the ski hats you like to make. Personally, I like to associate numbers with the feelings of, when was I was or will be at a given age. The number 18 is an easy one. That's the age when I went out into the world. By age 104 I hope to be an old, but happy great grandma. Many disciplines use memorable sentences to help students memorize concepts. The first letter of each word in the sentence is also the first letter of each word in a list that needs to be memorized. Medicine, for example, is laden with memorable mnemonics. Among the cleaner of which are: “Some Lovers Try Positions That They Can't Handle”, to memorize the names of the carpal bones of the hand. And “Old People from Texas Eat Spiders”, for the cranial bones. Time after time, these kinds of memory tricks prove helpful. If you're memorizing something commonly used, see whether someone has come up with a particularly memorable memory trick by searching it out online. Otherwise, try coming up with your own. The memory palace technique is a particularly powerful way of grouping things you want to remember. It involves calling to mind a familiar place. Like the layout of your house, and using it as a sort of a visual notepad where you can deposit the concept images that you want to remember. All you have to do is call to mind the place you're familiar with. Your home, your route to school, or your favorite restaurant and voila in the blink of an imaginative eye this becomes the memory palace that you'll use as your notepad. The memory palace technique is useful for remembering unrelated items, such as a grocery list. Milk, bread, eggs. To use the technique, you might imagine a gigantic bottle of milk just inside your front door. The bread plopped on the couch and a cracked egg dribbling off the edge of the coffee table. In other words, you'd imagine yourself walking through a place you know well, coupled with shockingly memorable images of what you want to remember. If you're studying Finance, Sociology, Chemistry or what have you, and you have lists to remember, you could use this same approach. The first time you do this, it will be slow. It takes a bit of time to conjure up a solid mental image. But the more you do it, the quicker it becomes. One study showed that a person using the Memory Palace technique could remember more than 95% of a 40 to 50 item list after only one or two practice mental walks, where the items were placed on the grounds of the local university. In using the mind this way, memorization can become an outstanding exercise in creativity that simultaneously build neural hooks for even more creativity. Purists might sniff that using oddball memorization gimmicks isn't really learning. But researchers show that students who use this kind of tricks outperform those who don't. In addition, imaging research on how people become experts, shows that such memory tools speed up the acquisition of both chunks and big picture templates. Helping transform novices to semi experts much more quickly, even in a matter of weeks. Memory tricks allow people to expand their working memory with easy access to long term memory. What's more, the memory process itself becomes an exercise in creativity. The more you memorize using these innovative techniques, the more creative you become. This is because you're building these wild, unexpected possibilities for future connections early on. Even as you're first internalizing the ideas, the more you practice this type of memory muscle so to speak, the more easily you'll be able to remember. Where at first it may take 15 minutes to build an evocative image for an equation and embed it say, in the kitchen sink of your memory palace, it can later take only minutes or seconds to perform a similar task. You'll also realize that as you begin to internalize key aspects of the material, taking a little time to commit the most important points to memory, you come to understand it much more deeply. The formulas will mean far more to you, than they would if you simply looked them up in a book. And you'll be able to sling those formulas around much more proficiently on tests and in real world applications. You may say, well, you're just not that creative, an equation or theory could hardly have its own grandiose motivations or persnickeity emotional needs to help you understand and remember it. But always remember, your childlike creativity is still there inside you. You just need to reach out to it. I'm Barbara Oakley. Thanks for learning how to learn.
在这段视频中，我们将更深入地探讨记忆。记忆的一个关键是创建有意义的组，以简化材料。例如，如果你想记住四种可以驱赶吸血鬼的植物：大蒜、玫瑰、山楂和芥末。它们的首字母组合成GRHM，所以你只需用一个“格雷厄姆饼干”的形象来记忆。这比起仅仅记数字，要容易得多。比如，1965年可能是你某个亲戚出生的年份，或者你可以将数字与你熟悉的事件关联起来。例如，11.0秒是100米短跑的好成绩，或者75可能是你喜欢制作滑雪帽时需要的针数。

我个人喜欢将数字与我在某个特定年龄时的感觉联系起来。数字18很容易记住，因为那是我走入社会的年龄。希望到104岁时，我能成为一个快乐的曾外祖母。许多学科使用记忆句子来帮助学生记忆概念。句子中每个单词的首字母也对应需要记忆的列表中的每个单词。医学中有很多这样的记忆法，其中一些比较简洁的句子是：“Some Lovers Try Positions That They Can't Handle”，用于记忆手部的腕骨，以及“Old People from Texas Eat Spiders”，用于记忆颅骨。

这些记忆技巧经常证明是有帮助的。如果你正在记忆某个常用的内容，可以在线查找是否有人提出过特别有趣的记忆技巧。否则，尝试自己想出一些。记忆宫殿技术是一种特别有效的记忆分组方法。它涉及召唤一个熟悉的地方，比如你家的布局，并把它作为一种视觉笔记本，用于存储你想要记住的概念图像。只需想象你熟悉的地方，比如你的家、上学的路线或你最喜欢的餐厅，这样一来，它就成了你用作笔记本的记忆宫殿。

这个技术对记忆无关紧要的项目非常有用，比如购物清单：牛奶、面包、鸡蛋。你可以想象在前门内有一瓶巨大的牛奶，面包放在沙发上，而一个裂开的鸡蛋正从咖啡桌边缘滴落。换句话说，你会想象自己走过一个熟悉的地方，同时伴随着令人震惊的图像来帮助记忆。如果你在学习金融、社会学、化学等，并有列表需要记住，你可以使用这种方法。

第一次使用时，这个过程会比较慢，因为需要一些时间来召唤一个稳固的心理图像。但你越是练习，速度会越快。一项研究显示，使用记忆宫殿技术的人在仅一次或两次心理练习中，就能记住40到50个项目中的95%以上。通过这种方式使用大脑，记忆不仅可以成为创造力的出色锻炼，同时也为更多的创造力建立神经连接。

一些纯粹主义者可能会认为使用奇特的记忆技巧并不算真正的学习，但研究表明，使用这种技巧的学生表现优于那些不使用的人。此外，关于人们如何成为专家的成像研究表明，这些记忆工具能加快学习块和整体模板的获取，帮助初学者在几周内更快地转变为半专业水平。记忆技巧使人们能够扩展他们的工作记忆，并轻松访问长期记忆。更重要的是，记忆过程本身也成为创造力的锻炼。

通过这些创新技术进行记忆，能增强你在未来建立意外连接的能力。起初，建立一个生动的图像可能需要15分钟，但随着练习，这个时间可以缩短到几分钟甚至几秒钟。你会发现，当你开始内化材料的关键方面时，花一点时间将最重要的点记住，会使你对内容理解得更深入。公式对你来说意义重大，而不是简单地查阅书籍，你在考试和实际应用中也能更熟练地运用这些公式。

你可能会说，你的创造力不强，一个方程或理论根本没有宏伟的动机或情感需求来帮助你理解和记忆。但请始终记住，你内心的童年创造力仍然存在。你只需要去触及它。我是芭芭拉·奥克利，感谢你学习如何学习。



### 3.11 Summary Week 3

Learning to use your memory in a more disciplined yet creative manner helps you learn to focus your attention, even as you create wild diffuse connections that build stronger memories. Here are the key ideas about memory we've covered:
学习以更有纪律且创造性的方式使用记忆可以帮助你集中注意力，同时创造出丰富多样的连接，从而建立更强的记忆。以下是我们讨论的记忆的关键概念：
