### 1-1 Introduction – About Mindshift

I'm Barbara Oakley. And I'm Terry Sejnowski. Welcome to Mindshift. We created the MOOC: Learning How to Learn which to our surprise became the world's largest and most popular MOOC, to give you the specifics about how to learn more effectively. In this MOOC, we're going to grow things even bigger, so you can learn not only more effectively but more broadly. In other words, we will help you to develop a learning lifestyle. Who you are today, is not the same person who went to sleep last night. We now know, that your experiences during the day are integrated into your brain circuits while you are asleep. So, that you wake up in the morning, your brain is updated.
我是芭芭拉·奥克利。我是特里·塞诺夫斯基。欢迎来到《思维转变》。我们创建了慕课课程《学习如何学习》，令我们惊讶的是，它成为了全球最大、最受欢迎的慕课，旨在为您提供更有效学习的具体方法。在这个慕课中，我们将进一步拓展，让您不仅能更有效地学习，还能更广泛地学习。换句话说，我们将帮助您培养一种学习的生活方式。您今天的自己，已经不同于昨晚入睡时的自己。我们现在知道，白天的经历会在您睡觉时整合进大脑回路。所以，当您早晨醒来时，大脑已经得到了更新。

You can take advantage of this to become a different person than you were last week, or last month, or last year because you brain is different and you can guide the process. To begin with, I'm going to tell you about one of the worst things that's ever happened to me. Something that cause one of my biggest most embarrassing failures. . Yes, everybody, including me, has failures and false starts. Now, you may know that I basically flunked my way through elementary, middle and High school Math and Science. When I was young, the only thing I thought I could do. The only thing I was interested in was trying to learn a new language. Any new language since all I spoke was English. If you speak English as an additional language you are so lucky. So, that's why I enlisted in the army right out of High school, because the military would actually pay me to learn a new language. Although, I wasn't of Russian heritage, I decided to learn Russian, and I did learn Russian. The army even gave me a scholarship, and I got my first degree, a bachelors degree in slavic languages and literature. I was all set, as a language expert I expected to go into military intelligence. To use my skills, that was pretty much the only place my new language skills could be used.
您可以利用这一点，让自己变得与上周、上个月甚至去年的自己不同，因为您的大脑已经有所不同，您可以引导这一过程。首先，我想告诉您我经历过的最糟糕的一件事。那是导致我一次最大、最尴尬失败的事情。是的，每个人，包括我自己，都经历过失败和不顺利的开端。您可能知道，我基本上在小学、初中和高中的数学和科学课程中一路挂科。小时候，我唯一认为自己能做的、唯一感兴趣的就是学习一门新语言。任何一种新语言，因为我只会说英语。如果您把英语作为一门额外的语言来学，您真的很幸运。这就是为什么我高中毕业后直接参军，因为军队会支付我学习一门新语言的费用。尽管我并不是俄裔，但我决定学习俄语，而且我确实学会了俄语。军队甚至还给了我一份奖学金，我获得了第一个学位——斯拉夫语言文学的学士学位。我万事俱备，作为一名语言专家，我希望进入军事情报部门，运用我的技能，因为那几乎是唯一可以用到我新语言技能的地方。

Didn't happen, it like. Really didn't happen, they only put me into the signal corps. That meant, that I would be responsible for all sorts of electrical communication systems. From cables, and telephones, to advanced electrical switching systems. I was horrified. And it wasn't like I was some kind of brilliant, intellectual superstar who turned it all around and charged ahead to immediately excel anyway. In fact, I graduated last in my class in signal officer training school. I didn't even know what a volt was, much less than how a telephone worked. Here I was stalking a field I hated, doing a job I couldn't really understand. I was so terrible at my job, that we all eventually agreed that I should move into something far less technical. Those four years as a military officer were very difficult. I was lonely, stationed thousands of miles from my home and family. And well, I was a failure. What's funny about this, is that it's actually one of the best things that's ever happened to me.
事情并没有如我所愿，真的完全没有如我所愿。他们只把我分配到了信号兵部队。这意味着我要负责各种电气通信系统，从电缆和电话到先进的电气交换系统。我当时非常害怕。而且我并不是那种能够迅速改变现状、马上表现出色的聪明人。事实上，我在信号官训练学校里是全班最后一名毕业的。我甚至不知道伏特是什么，更不用说电话是如何工作的了。我就这样陷入了一个我讨厌的领域，做着一份我根本无法理解的工作。我在这份工作上表现得太糟糕了，以至于我们最终一致认为我应该转到技术性较低的岗位上去。这四年的军官生活非常艰难，我很孤独，被派驻在离家和家人几千英里的地方。而且，说实话，我是个失败者。可笑的是，这竟然成为我生命中最好的经历之一。

Believe it or not, I'm now a professor of engineering and I love my job. In this MOOC, we're going to talk about what I learned on the path to my change in thinking, my mind shift. We're going to talk not only about some of the changes I've experienced. And inspirational and insightful stories of other people's growth and change. But, we're also going to be exploring what science is telling us about how we can change.
信不信由你，我现在是一名工程学教授，而且我非常热爱这份工作。在这门慕课中，我们将讨论我在思维转变的过程中学到的东西，我的心态转变。不仅会谈到我自己经历的一些变化，还会分享其他人在成长和改变中的一些鼓舞人心且富有见解的故事。同时，我们还将探讨科学告诉我们的关于如何改变的知识。

This course is about how you can do and be much more than you ever might think. It's about how you can grow from failure to success. It's about how you can broaden your horizons and be more than you ever thought you could be, no matter what your age, or your past. It's about how you'll feel better and be healthier because of a learning lifestyle. And, it's about how to look around you, at what you're learning, in your place, in what's unfolding in the society around you. So you can be what you want to be, given the real world constraints that life puts on us all. No, this isn't a course based on magic, where we promise to somehow turn you into a genius and make all your wishes for learning and careers come true. But you'll see, that by using certain mental tricks and insights you can often learn more and do more. Far more than you might have ever dreamed, we'll talk about what science tells us about how we learn and change. We'll build on what you already know to take your life's learning fantastic new directions. You might think, well, I've taken aptitude tests. The experts are telling me what I should be doing. And besides, I can feel inside what I'm good at.
这门课程是关于如何超越自我，成就远超您想象的更多可能。它会告诉您如何从失败走向成功，如何开阔视野，超越自我，无论年龄或过去如何。课程将帮助您通过一种学习的生活方式获得更好的状态和更健康的生活。同时，它也会引导您观察周围、观察您在所在位置学到的东西，观察在您身边的社会中正在发生的事。这样，您就可以在现实生活的各种限制下，实现自己想要的目标。不，这不是一门基于魔法的课程，我们不会承诺让您变成天才，或让所有学习和职业的愿望都实现。但您会发现，通过使用某些心智技巧和洞察力，您往往可以学得更多、做得更多，远超出您曾经的梦想。我们将讨论科学告诉我们的关于学习和改变的知识，并基于您已有的知识，将您的学习带向令人激动的新方向。您可能会想：“我已经做过能力测试了，专家告诉我该做什么。况且，我也能感觉到自己擅长什么。”

But remember, all those aptitude tests and internal feelings are just reflections of what you're good at right now. They don't give you a clue about how you can shift and shifting your thinking, mind shift is what this course is all about. Are you ready? Lets get started.
但请记住，所有这些能力测试和内心的感觉只是对您当前擅长事物的反映。它们并不能告诉您如何实现转变，而思维的转变正是这门课程的核心。您准备好了吗？让我们开始吧。



### 1-2 The Value of Being a Slow Learner

Have you ever watched as the teacher would ask a question in class and then before you could even understand what the question was about, some student already had their hand in the air with the answer? Some people just plain seem to have race car brains. They get to the finish line, they answer really, really fast. Other people like me have what you might call hiker brains. They get to the finish line but because they're walking, they get there much, much more slowly. With the race car driver, they do get to the finish line a lot faster, but everything goes by in a rush.  They're also on a set, smooth roadway. They know exactly where they're going. A hiker on the other hand, moves slowly. But while they're hiking, they can reach out. They can touch the leaves on the trees, smell the air, hear the birds. And they can easily veer off the expected path into places where people don't normally go.
您是否曾在课堂上看到老师提问时，自己还没理解问题，有的同学就已经举手回答了？有些人似乎天生拥有“赛车”般的大脑，他们迅速到达终点，回答得非常快。而像我这样的人，大脑更像是“徒步者”。我们也能到达终点，但因为我们走得慢，速度会慢得多。赛车手确实会更快到达终点，但一切都飞速掠过，而且他们行驶在一条平坦的道路上，知道自己要去哪里。而徒步者则缓慢前行，但在路上，他们可以伸手触摸树叶，闻到空气的气息，听到鸟鸣。他们还可以轻易偏离预定的路径，进入其他人通常不会走的地方。

The race car brain and the hiker brain, in other words have two completely different experiences. And even though the hiker brain may move much more slowly sometimes, because of how it works it can see more deeply. My hero in science is a man named Santiago Ramon y Cajal. Ramon y Cajal won the Nobel Prize in 1906, for his pioneering work in helping us understand the structure of the nervous system. Ramon y Cajal is considered the father of modern neuroscience. But here is where it gets really interesting, Ramon y Cajal was not a genius. He said so himself and he wasn't just being humble. However, Ramon y Cajal worked with geniuses. He found they often shared similar problems. For example, these geniuses with their race car brains  were used to jumping ahead to speedy conclusions. And when they were incorrect, they weren't use to changing their minds. So they keep charging ahead with the incorrect conclusion they jumped to, their super fast brains could easily devise justification. Because they weren't really looking to prove themselves wrong. Ramon y Cajal himself though had a persistent hiker type brain. He'd come up with a hypothesis and then he'd persistently check it out in a way that would reveal whether he was wrong. Instead of just trying to prove that he was right. If he was wrong, he changed his mind and flexibly try again. So was his persistence and his flexibility in the face of what the data was truly telling him that made him superstar researcher. It wasn't his genius. This kind of phenomena is seen in many different fields. For example, super smart people don't make very good hostage negotiators. Why? Because they go into the hostage situation with their own preconceived notions, which are sometimes dead wrong. And then when critical information does reveal itself, they can't flexibly change their mind and take advantage of that information. So if you have a race car brain just be aware, one of your biggest assets can become your biggest liability if you get too used to thinking you're always right, and that you're the smartest person around. And if you have a hiker type brain rejoice, there's much for you to contribute in the world with your slow, sometimes very unexpected way of approaching things.
换句话说，“赛车”型大脑和“徒步”型大脑会经历完全不同的体验。尽管徒步型大脑有时可能移动得更慢，但由于其运作方式，它能够看到更深层的内容。我的科学偶像是一位名叫圣地亚哥·拉蒙-卡哈尔的人。拉蒙-卡哈尔因其在帮助我们理解神经系统结构方面的开创性工作，于1906年获得了诺贝尔奖。他被誉为现代神经科学之父。但有趣的是，拉蒙-卡哈尔并不是天才，他自己也这么说过，而且这不是谦虚之词。然而，拉蒙-卡哈尔曾与许多天才共事，他发现他们往往有一些相似的问题。例如，这些拥有“赛车”型大脑的天才习惯于快速得出结论，而当他们的结论不正确时，他们往往不愿改变自己的想法，于是继续沿着错误的结论前进。凭借他们的快速思维，他们很容易为错误找到合理的解释，因为他们并不是在真正寻找证明自己错误的证据。

然而，拉蒙-卡哈尔本人的大脑则是坚持不懈的徒步型。他提出一个假设，然后执着地验证它是否正确，而不仅仅是想证明自己是对的。如果发现自己错了，他会改变想法，灵活地重新尝试。正是这种坚持和面对数据的灵活性使他成为杰出的研究者，而非他的天才。类似的现象在许多领域都可以看到。例如，超级聪明的人并不是很好的人质谈判专家。为什么呢？因为他们带着固有的成见进入人质谈判，有时这些成见完全错误。当关键信息出现时，他们无法灵活地改变主意，从而错失利用这些信息的机会。

所以，如果您有“赛车”型大脑，请注意，当您过于习惯认为自己总是对的、是最聪明的那个人时，您的最大优势可能会成为最大的劣势。而如果您有徒步型大脑，请庆幸，您在这个世界上可以用您缓慢、有时非常出人意料的方式做出许多贡献。



### 1-3 From Passive to Active

The Fengjia Night Market in Taichung, Taiwan is really famous. They have just about everything you could imagine. One evening, you could have also found me there. I ended up at the stall that sold something called stinky tofu. I mean, stinky tofu is really stinky. The reality is, though, it tastes pretty good, kind of like a strong cheese. The thing is, you watching me tell you about stinky tofu is very different from you actually going to the famed Fengjia night market yourself and tasting stinky tofu. It's only when you actively do something yourself that you're learning really sticks. Trust me, I'll never forget about stinky tofu. You might think that taking an active approach to learning is totally obvious. But not only do students often not get this. Even professional teachers and professors themselves often don't get this, although it goes right to the heart of how we can most effectively learn and change. It's easy even for someone like me who knows better to sometimes take the easier path and avoid being active in what I'm trying to learn. Active learning applies to any kind of learning, whether it's learning about stinky tofu, leaning how to speak French, or learning to play the piano. It also applies to learning in math and science. This paper right here gathered together the results of some 200 studies on active learning in science, technology, engineering, and math programs. Turns out that one in three people drop out of these kinds of courses if they are taught in the traditional way with the professor droning away in the front of the classroom. But only one in five students drops from their course if it's taught with a professor using active learning, where the students get to actively work the problems themselves. In other words, the best teachers build breaks into their lectures so that students can actively work with the materials. Now, if you're a professor or teacher, and you think well, there's just no time for this just remember that speaking fast and covering a lot of material doesn't mean that the students are actually learning the material. There are guidelines that can help you cover the material even while your students are actively learning in your classroom.
台湾台中的逢甲夜市非常有名，几乎可以找到你能想象的一切。有一天晚上，你还会在那里找到我。我最终停在了一个卖“臭豆腐”的摊位前。臭豆腐的味道真的是很臭，不过实际上味道不错，有点像浓郁的奶酪。但关键是，你看我讲臭豆腐和你自己亲自去逢甲夜市尝臭豆腐的体验是完全不同的。只有当你亲身去体验时，学习才会真正深入人心。相信我，我永远不会忘记臭豆腐的味道。

您可能认为主动学习的方式显而易见，但实际上，学生往往没有意识到这一点。甚至很多专业的老师和教授也没有意识到，尽管这正是我们有效学习和改变的核心。即使是像我这样明白主动学习重要性的人，有时也会选择轻松的路径，避免主动参与学习。主动学习适用于任何学习，无论是了解臭豆腐，学习法语，还是学钢琴，也适用于数学和科学学习。

这里有一篇论文，汇集了约200项关于科学、技术、工程和数学项目中主动学习的研究结果。结果显示，如果用传统的教学方法，即教授在课堂前单调讲授内容，那么大约有三分之一的学生会退出这类课程。而如果教授使用主动学习的方法，让学生自己动手解决问题，那么仅有五分之一的学生会退课。换句话说，最好的教师会在讲课中设置间隙，让学生主动参与到材料的学习中。

如果您是教授或老师，觉得没有时间这样做，请记住：说得快、覆盖内容多并不代表学生真的学会了这些内容。课堂上有一些指导方针可以帮助您在确保学生主动学习的同时，顺利完成课程内容的讲授。

I once had a student who was flunking my engineering statistics class. When I questioned him about it, he said it was because his English was bad. He spoke English as an additional language. But actually his English was great. It turned out that he just wasn't taking the time to actively work through the problems himself. He was fooling himself. Looking at worked out solutions right in front of him and thinking he knew how to solve them himself. He also wasn't working actively with his team. Once we corrected that problem, and he started working actively with the problems himself, checking them with others, he began to do much better. As one of the artists for this MOOC told me, only watching tutorials and avoiding the actual practice is a big problem with art students who are trying to teach themselves. As another example, let's look at how I helped to construct the MOOC, Learning How to Learn. I'd never done any camera work or editing before. I watched YouTube clips to learn how to edit videos and that helped me to get started. But it was only when I started actively editing videos myself in conjunction with watching the YouTube instructions, that it really began to stick. One trick I've learned as a teacher is to deliberately put my hands behind my back when I'm trying to show something to students. It helps keep me from reaching out and doing whatever I'm trying to ask the student to do. For example, it's always tempting for me to write an equation or adjust the setting myself, because I already know it, and I can do it much more quickly. I even do this hands-behind-the-back technique as a mom and as a grandma. So as a learner, if someone tries to show you by writing or doing it themselves, try to gently push them away so that you are doing it. More generally do your best to take charge of whatever you're learning about to put a pen or pencil to paper to take quizzes, to do assignments, get your hands on it. So you can actively master the material yourself. Be an active group member in collaborative testing or any group project or assignment. In other words, test, test, test yourself all the time on anything you really want to learn. If the book's just open there in front of you or the video is just played right before you, you think you know it, but you don't. Only when you close the book or turn away from the video and test yourself by seeing if you can do it yourself, do you really know that you know it. Now, here comes the fade to white, you know what's coming.
我曾经有一个学生在我的工程统计课上挂科了。当我问他原因时，他说是因为他的英语不好，因为他把英语作为一门额外的语言来学。但实际上他的英语非常好。问题在于他没有花时间亲自去做题目，而是在自欺欺人。看着面前的解题步骤，觉得自己已经掌握了如何解题，但其实并没有。他也没有积极参与到小组合作中。纠正了这个问题后，他开始自己主动做题，与其他人一起检查，成绩就逐渐提高了。

这门慕课的一位艺术家告诉我，只看教程而不实际练习是艺术自学学生的一个大问题。另一个例子是，当我参与构建《学习如何学习》这门慕课时，我从未接触过摄像或剪辑工作。为了学习剪辑视频，我看了很多YouTube教程，这帮助我入了门。但只有当我在看YouTube教程的同时，自己动手实际剪辑视频时，技能才真正开始巩固。

作为教师，我学到的一个小技巧是，当我给学生展示某些东西时，会故意把手放在背后，这样我就不会情不自禁地亲自动手。比如，我总是很想自己去写一个方程或调整设置，因为我已经知道怎么做，而且可以更快完成。我甚至在当妈妈和奶奶时也使用这个“手放在背后”的方法。所以，作为学习者，如果有人想通过写或自己动手来示范，试着轻轻推开他们，让自己来动手。

更广泛地说，尽量掌控您所学的内容，动手写写画画，做测验，完成作业，亲自实践，以便自己主动掌握这些知识。在协作测试或任何小组项目或任务中，积极参与。在任何您真正想学的东西上，不断测试自己。如果书本打开在面前或视频播放在您眼前，您可能会觉得自己已经掌握了，但实际上并没有。只有当您合上书或背对视频，测试自己能否独立完成，您才真正知道自己掌握了。

现在画面慢慢变白了，您知道接下来会发生什么。

Okay, that in video quiz question was totally easy. But we put it there just because it helps reinforce a vital point and start an effective study habit in this MOOC. Actually, good quiz questions can be super helpful for tamping down your knowledge, which is why it helps to take quizzes. If you have one hour of studying versus one hour of taking a test, you'll actually learn far more when taking the test, even if you don't get the answers to the test, and even if you fail the test and don't know the answers. Well, at least then you know exactly what questions you want to find the answers to the next time you sit down to study. You can't actively do everything in every course all the time. From time to time, all of us especially me  are distracted by our wandering minds.  But the more you make use of active learning about the key aspects of a material, the better your ability to change your thinking to make a MindShift. It can take more effort to learn actively, so do not be surprised if your brain sometimes finds excuses to shy away from active learning. Incidentally, working with others is another way of grappling actively with the material, so that's why it can be so helpful to interact on the discussion forms. In fact, talking with others is probably more pleasant than any other kind of active learning. Anyway, all of this is what is really important for you to actively work through the exercises we're suggesting. And sometimes to interact with others to get the most out of this course, or to get the most of any subject you're trying to master. Incidentally, MOOCs like this one can be better than just watching a television show about what you're trying to learn. That's because MOOCs give you the opportunity to actively practice with the material, and interact with others, as well as just listen to your professor. So remember, in this course, and every subject or area where you really want to master the material, keep yourself working actively. I'm Barbara Oakley. Happy MindShift.
好吧，视频中的测验题确实非常简单。但我们之所以设置它，是因为它有助于强化一个重要的点，同时在这门慕课中培养有效的学习习惯。实际上，好的测验题可以帮助巩固知识，因此参与测验是有益的。如果你有一小时的学习时间和一小时的测验时间，那么即便没有测试答案，甚至没有通过考试，只要去参加测试，你学到的内容实际上会更多。至少，你会清楚下次学习时应该找到哪些问题的答案。

当然，不可能在每门课上时时刻刻都保持积极学习。我们所有人，尤其是我，有时会被自己的思绪分散注意力。但是越多地在学习材料的关键部分运用主动学习，你就越能改变思维，实现思维转变。主动学习确实需要更多努力，因此如果你的大脑有时找借口回避，不要感到意外。

顺便提一下，与他人一起学习也是主动学习的一种方式，这就是为什么在讨论区互动会非常有帮助。事实上，与他人交流可能是最愉快的主动学习方式。总之，重要的是要认真完成我们提供的练习，有时通过互动来充分利用这门课程，或是你试图掌握的任何学科内容。

顺便说一句，像这门慕课就比只看关于学习内容的电视节目更有效。因为慕课不仅可以让你听教授讲解，还能让你主动练习所学内容，并与他人互动。因此，请记住，在这门课程或任何你真正想要掌握的领域中，始终保持主动学习。我是芭芭拉·奥克利。祝你思维转变愉快！



### 1-4 The Value of Your Past

Thomas Kuhn was a detective. He wasn't your ordinary detective. For one thing, he'd gotten his doctorate in physics from Harvard University. For another, after he'd gotten his doctorate, he'd done a major mind shift and morphed to become a historian of science. He held professorships at the University of California, Berkeley, then at Princeton, and finally at MIT. Kuhn was interested in the process of how science unfolds. Is it just a steady accumulation of bits and pieces that gradually build our understanding of the real world? Or, is it more punctuated? A breakthrough here, a breakthrough there? Interestingly, Kuhn found there's a lot of what's called normal science. Normal science takes an idea or approach and fleshes it out to build our knowledge base. Normal science is like pottering along with Isaac Newton's theories about how the universe works. Or with the idea that stress causes gastric ulcers. An idea that virtually every scientist working in the field took for granted. But every once in a while, what can be called a paradigm shift happens. Basically someone takes the same information that everyone else sees, sort of like seeing a duck. See the beak right here? And they interpret it, they see it in a completely different way. They suddenly see, for example, that the duck can also be a rabbit. See how the rabbit ears point upwards?
托马斯·库恩是一位侦探。他并不是普通的侦探。首先，他从哈佛大学获得了物理学博士学位。其次，在拿到博士学位后，他进行了一个重大的思维转变，转而成为科学史学家。他先后在加州大学伯克利分校、普林斯顿大学和麻省理工学院担任教授。

库恩对科学的发展过程非常感兴趣。科学是否只是零碎知识的稳步积累，从而逐渐构建我们对现实世界的理解？或者，这一过程是更具间断性的？这里一个突破，那里一个突破？有趣的是，库恩发现科学中存在大量的“常规科学”。常规科学采用一种想法或方法，扩展细化，构建我们的知识体系。常规科学就像是沿用艾萨克·牛顿关于宇宙如何运行的理论，或者认为压力导致胃溃疡的观点——这是几乎所有该领域的科学家都视为理所当然的观点。

但偶尔会发生所谓的“范式转变”。基本上，有人用与他人相同的信息，却得出完全不同的解读，有点像看到一只鸭子。看到鸭子的喙了吗？他们从完全不同的角度解读这些信息，突然间发现，这只鸭子也可以是一只兔子。看看兔子的耳朵，是不是向上竖着？

So as science unfolds, there are periods of normal science. Expanding the knowledge base using normal methodology. Physicists might use Newton's laws to calculate the motion of the planets. Scientists might work to have a better understanding of the acidic environment of the stomach. Knowledge expands out in the usual way as scientists do their work. But, as science is marching placidly along someone comes along who's able to see things in a brand new way. A paradigm shift. For example, Einstein was able to see through the usual Newtonian physics, to view the universe in a different, more relativistic way. And Nobel Prize winner Barry Marshall famously gave himself an ulcer, along with some very bad breath, by drinking a concoction of the bacteria Helicobacter pylori. So that he could convince his critics that it was bacteria, not stress, that was the primary cause of ulcers. So indeed, the scientific process unfolds with punctuations. Periods of normal science that are then interrupted by a paradigm shift which shapes how normal science continues to unfold, until the next paradigm shift. And so on. Paradigm shifts allow us to have enormous new gains in our creative understanding of the world. So what kind of people make paradigm shifts? Those kinds of mind shifts that allowed them to see the world around them in new ways? Kuhn found that there were two types of mind shifters. One type was young people, people who hadn't yet been indoctrinated into seeing the world in the same way that everybody else did. With their youthful eyes, they can see with fresh perspectives. Now, if you don't qualify as a young person, you're probably thinking, that knocks me out then! I'm not in my teens or 20s, so no breakthroughs for me. But hang on. There was a second group of people. People who were older but who were just as innovative as those young people. These were people who had switched disciplines or careers. It was the change in focus, the career switch, that allowed the second older group to see with fresh eyes. Often, it allowed them to bring their seemingly unrelated prior knowledge to the table in new ways that helped them to innovate. These insights from science can also help us understand creativity and innovation in our everyday lives and careers. Let's take me. In my late teens and early 20s, I learned Russian. Then in my late 20s, I decided to start learning math and science. You might think that my time spent learning Russian was a waste. It wouldn't help once I switched my focus to engineering. But that's actually not at all true. Learning Russian gave me a lot of the insight about the learning process more generally. And I found that learning insight that I'd gained, transferred to help me be better in learning math and science. The same repetition, deliberate practice on the hard stuff, and flexible interleaving that helped me to successfully learn Russian and helped me to be successful when I started to begin to learn math and science. We see this phenomenon constantly in many fields. A background in sports can come in handy in a marketing career. Insights from a former career as an event planner can help you be a better software programmer. A hobby playing action-style video games can actually sharpen your mind and even your eyesight. Head towards the discussion forum after this video and tell others about the unexpected assets you've brought into your work from your past seemingly unconnected knowledge. Feel free to also tell others how you've broken through initial feelings of incompetence on your way to learning something new. As you post your own thoughts, you'll be surprised to find the fantastic and inspirational stories of others. Old or young, you may feel like you have a childlike incompetence when you're learning something new or you're changing disciplines or careers. This is very typical. But keep in mind that the feelings of incompetence will gradually pass. The creative power that you can bring to the table because of your willingness to change can be invaluable. It might even lead you to start a paradigm shift of your own.
随着科学的发展，会经历一段段的常规科学时期，通过常规方法扩展知识基础。物理学家可能会用牛顿定律来计算行星的运动，科学家可能会更深入地了解胃酸环境。知识按照通常的方式扩展，科学家们各司其职。然而，当科学平稳前进时，有人会以全新的方式看待事物，从而带来范式转变。例如，爱因斯坦超越了传统的牛顿物理学，以不同、更具相对性的视角观察宇宙。诺贝尔奖得主巴里·马歇尔则通过饮用幽门螺杆菌混合物给自己制造了胃溃疡并引发口臭，最终说服批评者相信溃疡的主要原因是细菌而非压力。

因此，科学的进展确实是间歇式的：一段常规科学时期后，范式转变随之而来，塑造了常规科学继续发展的方向，直到下一个范式转变出现。范式转变让我们在理解世界的创造力上获得巨大的进展。那么，究竟是什么样的人才会实现范式转变？库恩发现，有两类人会发生这种思维转变。第一类是年轻人，他们尚未被灌输固定的世界观，因此能以崭新的视角看待事物。如果您不属于年轻人，可能会想，那我没机会了！我已经不是青少年或二十多岁的人了，看来突破与我无缘。但别急，库恩还发现了第二类人，即那些年长但同样创新的人，他们是那些转换过学科或职业的人。正是这种焦点的转变、职业的切换，赋予了第二类年长者以全新的视角，使他们能够以新的方式将之前看似无关的知识运用到新的领域，帮助他们创新。

这些科学上的洞见也能帮助我们理解日常生活和职业中的创造力与创新。就以我为例。二十岁左右时我学习俄语，二十七八岁时决定开始学习数学和科学。您可能会认为学习俄语的时间浪费了，毕竟我转向工程后，俄语似乎毫无帮助。但事实上，俄语学习让我对学习过程有了更深的理解。我发现，这种学习洞见让我在学习数学和科学时也更为成功。学习俄语时用到的重复、对难题的刻意练习以及灵活的交错学习法，帮助我成功掌握了俄语，也同样帮助我在学习数学和科学时取得成功。

我们在很多领域都能看到这种现象。体育背景可能对营销事业有所帮助，前活动策划的经验可以让你成为更出色的软件程序员。玩动作类电子游戏的爱好甚至可以提高思维敏捷度和视力。在本视频结束后，前往讨论区，与他人分享您从过去看似无关的知识中带到工作的意外收获。也可以分享您如何克服最初的无力感，逐步学习新事物的经历。当您发表自己的想法时，会惊讶地发现其他人那些精彩且激励人心的故事。无论是年轻人还是年长者，在学习新东西、转换学科或职业时，都可能会感觉自己像孩子一样无力，这是很正常的。但请记住，这种无力感会逐渐消退。正因为您愿意改变，这种创造力将无比宝贵，甚至可能引领您发起自己的范式转变。



### 1-5 Mastery Learning

I started to try to take physics in my Senior year of high school, but I was flunking so badly, they finally took me out of the class. I stayed away from science and math as much as I possibly could. Why would I punish myself by trying to study subjects I obviously had no talent for? Of course, the fact that I'm now a professor of engineering tells you I was dead wrong about what I could develop a talent for. Some people do find some subjects easier to learn than others. But say, if your brother seems naturally smarter than you at math, this doesn't mean you can't learn the subject yourself. In fact, you may actually sometimes be even more creative than your brother with math, because you're using a different set of neural circuits than he is. When we go through school, we tend to focus on areas that we're thought to be good at. If we happen to find math easy and English more difficult, for example, we'll tend to take more math courses, if we can, and take fewer English courses. After all, taking English could hurt our grade point average. And if we're better at English, and not so good at math, we'll focus on English courses and skip the math. This means we tend to get more practice at what we're already kind of good at, so we get even better at it. But the flip side is, we don't get as much practice in other areas and so we tend to lag behind in them. And if we do go to college, where we have to pick a major, this tendency is sharpened even further. All this relates to a concept called mastery learning. In old-fashioned instruction, the kind you've probably experienced in school, all the students in a class are given the same amount of instruction time to learn the material. In mastery learning, on the other hand, it's understood that different students may need different amounts of instruction time and different amounts of practice in order to master the material, even though they all eventually do master the material. In fact, research is showing the value of mastery learning, where you can retake quiz variants over and over again, until you feel comfortable with the material. You can re-watch lectures if you need to, or even get different explanations of the material. This approach, as researchers are discovering, is one of the best methods for helping people to gain expertise, even with material they never thought they could learn before. There's all sorts of evidence of how, once you begin practicing in some area, your brain starts to develop the new neural architecture that supports your learning. I once met a taxi driver in London who'd been a complete failure in high school. But he'd spent several years studying for the London taxi driver's examination, which is a very intense test where thousands of different routes must be internalized.
我高中的最后一年曾试图学习物理，但成绩差得离谱，最终被迫退课。从那之后，我尽可能远离科学和数学。毕竟，为什么要去惩罚自己，尝试学习那些显然没有天赋的科目呢？当然，现在我是工程学教授，这也说明我对自己的潜力判断完全错了。有些人确实发现某些学科更容易学，但假如您的哥哥似乎在数学上天生比您聪明，这并不意味着您自己不能学好数学。事实上，您在数学上可能会比您的哥哥更有创造力，因为您使用的是不同的一组神经回路。

在学校，我们往往专注于自己被认为擅长的领域。例如，如果我们觉得数学简单而英语更难，我们会倾向于多选数学课，尽量少选英语课，毕竟，选英语可能会影响我们的平均成绩。而如果我们更擅长英语，不太擅长数学，就会集中选英语课，避开数学课。这意味着我们在已经有点擅长的领域获得更多练习机会，从而更加精通。然而，另一面是，我们在其他领域的练习较少，因此在这些领域往往会落后。如果我们上了大学，还要选择专业，这种趋势会进一步加剧。

这与一个叫做“掌握性学习”的概念有关。在传统的教学中，所有学生在同样的时间内接受相同的课程。而在掌握性学习中，不同的学生可能需要不同的学习时间和练习量来掌握知识，尽管最终他们都能掌握学习内容。研究表明，掌握性学习的价值显著，学生可以反复进行测验，直到对材料有信心为止；可以在需要时重看讲座，或获得不同的解释。研究人员发现，这种方法是帮助人们掌握专业知识的最佳方法之一，甚至包括他们原本认为无法学会的内容。

各种证据表明，一旦开始在某个领域进行练习，大脑就会开始构建新的神经结构来支持学习。我曾在伦敦遇到一位出租车司机，他在高中时完全失败，但却花了几年时间备考伦敦出租车司机考试，这是一项非常严格的考试，需要内化成千上万条不同的路线。

After he passed, he began realizing that his brain seemed different. He could focus and concentrate more effectively. And indeed, research has shown that by studying for the London taxi driver test and then actually practicing his spatial abilities as a driver, this fellow was able to fundamentally change his brain, increasing the size of his hippocampus, an important area in learning. What's great is that these new forms of online learning, such as MOOCs like this one, allow for mastery learning. In fact, you can actually even flunk classes completely and still turn out to be a successful learner. Pat Bowden, for example, is a retired bank officer from Queensland, Australia. Her husband mentioned MOOC-taking as a hobby for her retirement. Pat saw it as a chance to learn about and master subjects she hadn't been able to study when she was younger. I've always been interested in astronomy, so I decided to do an astronomy MOOC. Soon we were into forces, gravity, and sending rockets to Mars. By week two, I was lost. I hadn't done any physics for 40 years and failed the course. But it didn't stop me. Instead of complex calculations, I let the heavy physics flow right past as I chose which videos to watch purely for interest.
通过考试后，他开始意识到自己的大脑似乎有所不同。他能够更有效地集中注意力和专注。研究确实显示，通过备考伦敦出租车司机考试并在实际驾驶中锻炼空间能力，这位司机能够从根本上改变自己的大脑，扩大了学习中重要的大脑区域——海马体的大小。

令人欣喜的是，这些新的在线学习形式，比如这门慕课，可以实现掌握性学习。实际上，即使完全挂科，仍然可以成为成功的学习者。例如，帕特·鲍登是一位来自澳大利亚昆士兰的退休银行职员。她的丈夫提议慕课作为她退休后的一个兴趣爱好。帕特则把它看作学习和掌握自己年轻时无法学习的学科的机会。

“我一直对天文学感兴趣，所以我决定选修一门天文学慕课。很快我们就进入了力学、重力以及火箭发射到火星的内容。第二周我就迷失了，因为我已经40年没有学过物理，最终挂科了。但这并没有让我止步。我跳过了复杂的计算，让艰深的物理内容略过，挑选自己感兴趣的视频观看。”

It was enlightening to realize I didn't have to pass the course. I could still learn something from it.
意识到自己不一定要通过课程，也能学到东西，这种领悟让我受益匪浅。

Later I tried another astronomy course, and then another, and yes, finally I passed. Sometimes, I take a MOOC more than once to consolidate my knowledge. Completing a course is very fulfilling, but no one else needs to know if you give up on one.
后来我尝试了另一门天文学课程，然后再尝试了一门，最终，我通过了。有时我会多次参加同一门慕课来巩固知识。完成一门课程让人很有成就感，但如果选择放弃，没有人会知道。做笔记真的帮助我更好地理解课程内容并收获更多。

Taking notes really helps me understand and get more out of a course. So far, I've completed 71 MOOCs, and failed or not finished about 15 more. And Do Edmond Sanou is a third-year statistics student from Burkina Faso. Online classes are both interesting and relaxing. I choose when I want to take classes. I can also replay videos until I understand the key ideas. I can't do that with my teachers in a regular class. Online is the best way I found to learn new skills. Overall, then, it helps to remember that any kind of learning is a little bit like learning to drive a car. You may not have the abilities of Ayrton Senna, the brilliant Brazilian race car driver who, after a lot of practice, became one of the greatest Formula One drivers of all time. But that certainly doesn't mean that you can't or shouldn't learn to drive if you have the opportunity. Some people may take longer to learn to drive than others. But most people, including me, can learn to drive.
截至目前，我已经完成了71门慕课，还有大约15门挂科或未完成。而埃德蒙·萨努是一位来自布基纳法索的大三统计学学生。他表示：“在线课程既有趣又放松。我可以选择何时上课，还能反复播放视频，直到我理解关键内容。这是在普通课堂上无法做到的。在线学习是我发现的最佳新技能学习方式。”
总的来说，记住任何学习都像学习开车有帮助。您可能没有像巴西赛车手艾尔顿·塞纳那样的天赋，经过大量练习，他成为了有史以来最伟大的一级方程式赛车手之一。但这绝不意味着，如果有机会，您不能或不应该学习驾驶。一些人可能比其他人花更长时间学习驾驶，但大多数人，包括我在内，都可以学会驾驶。

And you can use those driving skills to drive to some wonderful places. Learning is for everyone, and online learning makes some of the best approaches to learning, like mastery learning, much easier.
您可以利用这些驾驶技能去往美好的地方。学习适合每一个人，而在线学习使得最佳的学习方法，例如掌握性学习，变得更为便捷。



### 1-6 Focused Versus Diffuse – Furthering Your Understanding

In our previous MOOC, Learning How to Learn, we described the two very different modes of operation of the brain, focused and diffuse. Focused, if you'll remember, is when you're concentrating. Diffuse is when you're not thinking about anything at all in particular. We used a pinball machine metaphor to explain these two different modes. In the focused mode, the rubber bumpers of the brain were closer together, while in the diffuse mode, they were farther apart. Your thoughts could bounce further, by analogy, this is why when you're focusing hard on a problem, you sometimes get stuck within the tight spaces of the bumpers. And you can't see another, better way to approach the problem.
在我们之前的慕课《学习如何学习》中，我们描述了大脑的两种截然不同的运作模式——专注模式和发散模式。还记得，专注模式是指当您集中注意力时的状态，而发散模式则是当您没有特别去思考任何事时的状态。我们用弹球机的比喻来解释这两种模式。在专注模式下，大脑的橡胶挡板距离较近，而在发散模式下，挡板距离较远。您的思维可以更远地跳跃。正因如此，当您专注于一个问题时，有时会被挡板之间的狭窄空间困住，从而无法找到另一种更好的解决方法。

We also described the importance of developing a library of neural chunks. Well practiced, mental patterns, that you can easily draw into your mind. This is all part of the process of gaining expertise in a subject. Metaphors and analogies can make learning super simple. Remember, these metaphors and analogies often just serve to give us a sense of key ideas. Whenever we've reached the limits of an analogy for helping us to understand something, we can always just throw that analogy away and pick up a new one. Of course, there's often many different analogies that we can use to explain any concept. So let's review, while having a little fun by describing the focus and diffuse modes using some different analogies. It turns out that your brain puts its energy, for the most part, into either the focused mode or the diffuse mode. It can't be in both modes at same time, not unless you're ingesting certain forms of mushrooms and we're certainly not suggesting you do that. Focused mode is what happens when you concentrate, it turns on virtually instantly. Diffuse mode, on the other hand, is when you're not concentrating on anything. Instead, it's when your thoughts are moving randomly, like when you're sitting on a bus, standing in a shower or going for a walk. Diffuse mode kind of sneaks up on you, you're often not aware of having fallen into the diffuse mode. So, let's look a little more closely at what's going on in the focused versus the diffuse modes. Here's a top scale view looking down onto your brain, you can see the little ears right here, and the nose is on the top. And we know that the brain is kind of like a set of networks, this is the focused mode network. Look how small those little meshes are, these are sometimes called task positive networks. Because different parts of the networks are activated depending on what task you're working on. Multiplication say, versus conjugating a verb in Spanish or kicking a soccer ball. What we call the diffuse mode is like a different set of networks, with much bigger meshes. Diffuse mode is actually a catchall term that we use to signify any of the neural resting states. The most prominent resting state, incidentally, is called the default mode network. The thing about the diffuse mode, is that it helps us to make these intuitive leaps, connections between new ideas you didn't realize were connected. The diffuse mode has another purpose as well, it's the mode we fall into when we're trying to consolidate and understand new information. We'll get to that in just a minute, the diffuse mode only turns on when you aren't thinking about anything in particular. So you can't just concentrate and turn it on like you can with the focused mode. But the relaxed, diffuse mode, is the mode that often does that background processing that helps us solve difficult problems and understand difficult concepts. This is why, when you're concentrating intently on something, and you find yourself getting really frustrated, the best thing you can do is often to get your concentration off what you're trying to understand. Getting your attention off the topic helps open up the very different diffuse mode networks, and lets your brain find new paths for thinking about what you're trying to understand. Then, when you later return to focusing, you'll find yourself in a better place, mentally. The problem or the concept will suddenly start to make sense. Incidentally, the habit of writing down a problem that you're trying to solve before going to bed will help you calm your mind and let your nighttime brain think of a solution. There's actually another, very different way, to think about focused and diffuse modes and that's using something called an excavator. When you focus on something, your mind is in receiving mode, information is pouring in. When you're in diffuse mode, on the other hand, your brain is turning around, so to speak, and placing that new information in other parts of your brain. Organizing and making sense of the new material, you can only be in one mode at the same time. The inputting focused mode or the organizing, diffuse mode, where the brain is consolidating that information. This is why it's really important to take little study breaks, and give yourself time where you're not focusing on the material at hand. The little break is what helps the brain consolidate the new information so it can later think more creatively about it. Go ahead, take a little break now, try to move around a little while you do it. You'll be surprised at how much it refreshes you.
我们还描述了建立神经“块”库的重要性。这些是您可以轻松调取的熟练心智模式，是掌握学科知识过程中不可或缺的一部分。隐喻和类比能够让学习变得极其简单。请记住，这些隐喻和类比通常只是帮助我们理解关键概念的工具。当某个类比不再适用于帮助我们理解某件事时，我们可以抛弃它，换一个新的。当然，任何概念往往都有许多不同的类比可供选择。

那么，让我们在轻松的气氛中，用不同的类比来回顾专注模式和发散模式。大脑的能量大多集中在专注模式或发散模式中，两者不能同时存在，除非您摄取了某些特定的蘑菇，而我们当然不建议您这么做。专注模式是在您集中注意力时开启的，几乎瞬间启动。而发散模式则是在您没有特别专注于任何事物时，大脑的思维随意流动，比如坐在公交车上、站在淋浴间里或散步时。发散模式通常悄然启动，您可能并未察觉到自己进入了发散模式。

现在，让我们更深入地了解专注模式和发散模式发生了什么。从顶视角来看大脑，就像一组网络，这是专注模式网络。注意这些小网格的细密程度，这些有时被称为“任务积极网络”，因为不同的任务会激活网络的不同部分，例如乘法计算、西班牙语动词的变位或踢足球。

我们所说的发散模式则像是另一组网格，网格更大。发散模式实际上是一个总称，表示所有神经的静息状态。其中最显著的静息状态被称为默认模式网络。发散模式的特点在于，它帮助我们进行直觉跳跃，连接那些看似不相关的新想法。发散模式还有另一个作用，即帮助我们整合和理解新信息。发散模式只在您没有特别专注于某事时启动，因此不能像专注模式那样通过集中注意力来激活它。但放松的发散模式常常会进行背景处理，帮助我们解决难题和理解复杂概念。

因此，当您在集中精力学习某件事并感到十分沮丧时，最好的方法往往是转移注意力。将注意力从问题上移开有助于激活发散模式网络，让大脑找到新的路径去思考所学内容。之后当您再次回到专注状态时，您会发现自己在精神上更有准备，问题或概念突然变得清晰了。顺便提一下，在入睡前写下您正在努力解决的问题可以帮助您平静下来，并让夜间的大脑思考解决方案。

另一个思考专注和发散模式的方式是使用“挖掘机”的比喻。当您专注于某件事时，大脑处于接收模式，信息源源不断地流入。而在发散模式中，您的大脑则开始“转身”，将新信息分配到大脑的其他部分，整理并理解这些新材料。您一次只能处于一种模式中——专注模式或组织整合的发散模式，即大脑在整合信息。这就是为什么需要小小的学习休息时间，给自己一个不专注于材料的片刻。这个小休息能帮助大脑巩固新信息，从而在之后更具创造性地思考内容。

现在就稍作休息吧，活动一下身体。您会惊讶于这种方式带来的清新感。



### 1-7 Should You Listen to Music When You're Studying?

What would you like research to tell you? Would you like it to say it's okay to listen to music when you're studying? Or would you rather that research would find that it's better not to listen to music when you're studying?
你希望研究告诉你什么？是希望研究表明学习时听音乐是可以的，还是希望研究发现不听音乐更好呢？

Let's pause for a moment to take a survey and see what you think.
让我们先暂停片刻，进行一项调查，看看您的看法。

I have a little insight for you. Whatever you wanted that research to tell you, whether it was to listen to music or not to listen to music, you can find research that will back you up. Here's what we do know. If the music is fast and loud, it disrupts reading comprehension,
in part because you use some of the same areas of the brain to process music as you do to process language. Also, music with lyrics is more distracting than music without lyrics. On the other hand, researchers have found that if you're listening to a favorite style of music it could enhance your studies. Or if it's something you don't like it could detract. In the final analysis, all this means that when it comes to music you should use commonsense and discover what works best for you.
我这里有一个小见解。无论您希望研究支持听音乐还是不听音乐，您都可以找到相关的研究支持您的立场。已知的是，如果音乐快而响，会影响阅读理解，部分原因是处理音乐和语言的大脑区域有重叠。此外，带歌词的音乐比无歌词的音乐更容易分散注意力。另一方面，研究发现，如果您在听自己喜欢的音乐风格，可能有助于学习，而如果是您不喜欢的音乐，可能会产生反效果。总之，关于音乐的问题，应当用常识判断，找出最适合自己的方法。



### 1-8 Learning Something Hard? The Coffee Shop Trick

When we're studying we often drink caffeine, which enhances focus by diminishing the day dreaming alpha waves in our brain.
学习时，我们经常会饮用含咖啡因的饮品，咖啡因通过减少大脑中的白日梦阿尔法波来增强专注力。

This effect is strongest for about an hour after drinking a cup of coffee or tea. Although the energizing can persist about eight hours, which is why it's sometimes better to avoid that evening cup of coffee. But when you're doing something that is cognitively difficult, coffee isn't the only booster. You're often subconsciously using other tricks to increase your focus. For example, if you're trying to remember something, you tend to avert your gaze. Which avoids overloading your working memory with unnecessary extra visual information from your environment. Even just closing your eyes can help you ignore distractors when you're trying to bring something to mind. Let's be honest here. Memorizing comes more easily for some people than others. Researchers still aren't quite sure why, although there's some evidence that having the right genes helps. But believe it or not, being a good memorizer can cause problems. In medical schools for example, when there's a big anatomy test, ordinary medical students spend weeks preparing. They will practice over and over again to memorize thousands of terms in their related functions. Gifted memorizers, on the other hand, can procrastinate until just a few days before the test, spend a few hours glancing over the material, and they can still do well. However, when these same memorizing aces are faced with a different type of medical school exam, for example a test related to how the heart functions, they find that just a few hours of last minute cramming just don't cut it. Medical school advisors can sometimes be startled to find these seemingly star students flunking certain sections of the curriculum. It seems that quickly memorizing anatomical terms related to the heart doesn't allow you to understand and answer questions about the heart's complex function. This is a reminder that simple focused concentration in memorization often isn't enough when we're trying to understand a complicated issue. It takes time to understand complicated systems. Whether we're trying to understand how to put together a new lighting rig,  figuring out a pumping system of a human heart, or analyzing the multifaceted causes of World War II. To untangle such complicated subjects, we often need to alternate a tight focus on the issue at hand with steps back to look at the bigger picture. Our need for occasional distraction during any given learning session may arise from these competing type focus versus big picture kinds of needs. Basically, it's focused versus diffuse modes. The focus mode is primarily centered in the prefrontal cortex, the front part of the brain. The diffuse mode, on the other hand, involves a network connecting more wide spread areas of the brain. The more extensive nature of diffused thinking is why it's often related to the unexpected connections that lie at the heart of creativity. Activities involving the diffuse mode like walking, or riding a bus, relaxing, or falling asleep, are more likely to lead you to creative ideas that can seem to arise from nowhere. If we're in a very quiet environment, that quietness can hype up the focused mode attention circuits while simultaneously deactivating the diffuse mode. This is why quiet environments are ideal when we're doing something that demands full concentrated attention, like doing our taxes or working on a difficult problem on a test. But sometimes we're trying to understand bigger picture sorts of issues like cardiac function or computer network connectivity. In that case, a little sporadic noise, like a snippet of conversation with or the clatter of dishes in the back round of a coffee shop can help. This is because that bit of noise temporarily allows the longer range diffuse network to pop up. So, we briefly get a new perspective. This is so effective that there are even apps with coffee shop sounds. In the discussion forum, you might want to describe some of your favorite ideas for places to study and unexpected apps that have helped your learning. Of course, there can come a point when there's just too much noise, which can keep you from concentrating at all. Finding a good learning environment can take a bit of exploration. But that's good because it keeps you from getting too accustomed to any one study place, which can make your learning more effective.
喝一杯咖啡或茶后，咖啡因的效果在一小时左右最为显著。尽管提神作用可以持续约八小时，这也是为何有时晚上最好避免咖啡。但在处理认知难度较高的任务时，咖啡并不是唯一的提神剂。我们通常会下意识地运用其他技巧来提高专注力。例如，当我们试图记住某些内容时，会习惯性地转移视线，以避免环境中不必要的额外视觉信息给工作记忆带来负担。甚至闭上眼睛也有助于排除干扰，更容易回忆所需内容。

让我们坦诚一些，记忆力对某些人来说更为轻松。研究人员尚不完全清楚原因，但有证据表明，遗传基因可能起作用。然而，优秀的记忆力也可能带来问题。在医学院，当解剖学考试临近时，普通医学生会花数周反复练习，以记住成千上万的术语及其功能。但天赋超群的记忆高手可能会拖延到考试前几天，仅用几小时快速浏览材料，并且仍然考得不错。然而，当这些记忆高手面对另一种类型的考试时，比如心脏功能相关的测试，他们会发现匆忙记忆几小时并不奏效。医学院的导师们有时会惊讶地发现，这些看似优异的学生在某些课程上挂科。看来，快速记住心脏相关的解剖学术语并不能帮助理解和解答心脏复杂功能的问题。

这提醒我们，在尝试理解复杂问题时，单靠专注于记忆并不够。理解复杂系统需要时间，不论是学习如何搭建新的灯光装置、研究人类心脏的泵送系统，还是分析二战的多方面原因。要解开这些复杂主题，我们通常需要在紧盯问题细节与退后看全局之间交替进行。学习过程中偶尔分心的需求，可能正源于专注与大局视角之间的竞争需求。

基本上，这就是专注模式和发散模式。专注模式主要集中在大脑的前额皮质，而发散模式涉及一个连接大脑更多区域的网络。发散性思维的更广泛特性，使其常常与创造力核心的意外联系相关。发散模式活动如散步、乘公交车、放松或入睡，更可能带来似乎凭空而来的创意。在非常安静的环境中，这种寂静会加强专注模式的注意力回路，同时抑制发散模式。这就是为什么安静的环境非常适合需要全神贯注的任务，例如报税或应对测试中的难题。

但有时我们在试图理解更宏观的问题，例如心脏功能或计算机网络连接，此时一点断续的噪音——如咖啡馆里的对话片段或餐具碰撞声，反而有帮助。这是因为这种噪音可以暂时激活长距离的发散网络，使我们从新的视角看待问题。这一方法非常有效，甚至出现了带有咖啡馆背景音的应用程序。

在讨论区中，您可以分享您喜欢的学习场所和对您学习有帮助的意想不到的应用程序。当然，噪音过多也会影响注意力，找到合适的学习环境可能需要探索。不过，这种探索有益于避免过于依赖同一个学习地点，使学习更有效。


### 1-9 Your environment affects who you are

What do you feel when you walk into a cathedral?
当你走进一座大教堂时，你会有什么感觉？

Light is streaming in through the stained glass. The ceiling soars above your head.Cavernous echoes create a sense of a vast space.It smells old. Compared to the world outside, your brain tells you that this space is sacred.It makes you feel different. It elicits different thoughts. You are different person inside the cathedral.
阳光透过彩色玻璃倾泻而入，天花板在头顶高耸，空旷的回声营造出一种广阔的空间感，弥漫着历史的气息。和外面的世界相比，你的大脑告诉你，这里是一个神圣的地方。这让你感到不同，唤起了不同的思绪。在教堂里，你仿佛成了另一个人。

Now walk in to a stadium filled with 100,000 fans for a football game. The sights are brighter than outside the stadium, and the sounds are much louder.  The crowd roars when a goal is stored.You are caught up in the emotional moment. This is why we still go to live sporting events, even though the camera angles are much, much better on TV.
现在，走进一个容纳十万名球迷的体育场，观看一场足球比赛。场内的光线比场外更亮，声音也更响亮。当进球得分时，观众欢呼沸腾。你被这情绪化的时刻所感染。这就是我们仍然亲临体育赛事的原因，尽管电视上的镜头视角更加完美。

These experiences have an impact on your brain. Whether you immerse yourself in a quiet, contemplative environment, or a hyper-stimulated one, you are changing your thought patterns, and that changes your brain. Different emotional states, different memories, different brain. Is your environment helping you to achieve our goals? Sometimes even small changes in your environment can lead to big differences over time.
这些体验对大脑有着深刻影响。无论你沉浸在安静、沉思的环境中，还是在高度刺激的环境中，你都在改变你的思维模式，这也会改变你的大脑。不同的情绪状态、不同的记忆，意味着不同的大脑。你的环境是否在帮助你实现目标？有时，即使环境中微小的变化，随着时间推移也会带来巨大的差异。

The Salk Institute for Biological Studies where I work is a special place. As you approach the Salk Institute from outside, it looks like a concrete fortress. But as you enter the central courtyard, there is a dramatic change. A broad expands of travertine stretches out to the Pacific Ocean with towers rising along the sides, anchoring the other worldly space. The Salk Institute was designed by Louis Khan, a famous architect working together with Jonas Salk, who invented the vaccine that cured polio in the 1950s. The building is an architectural landmark. Jonas was a medical researcher but he founded an institute whose mission was basic science. Our motto, cures begin here. Salk reinvented himself as a visionary, and wanted his institute to inspire the researchers working there to make important scientific breakthroughs, and we have.
我工作的索尔克生物研究所是一个特殊的地方。从外面看，索尔克研究所像一座混凝土要塞，但当你走入中央庭院时，景象骤然一变。宽阔的石灰华庭院延伸向太平洋，两侧的塔楼耸立，支撑起这一片超凡脱俗的空间。索尔克研究所由著名建筑师路易斯·康设计，他与乔纳斯·索尔克——1950年代发明治愈脊髓灰质炎疫苗的医学研究人员——携手合作完成了这座建筑。这栋建筑是建筑史上的一座地标。乔纳斯虽然是医学研究人员，却创立了以基础科学为使命的研究所。我们的座右铭是：“治愈从这里开始。”索尔克重新定义了自己，成为了一个有远见的开拓者，期望他的研究所能激励在此工作的研究人员取得重要的科学突破，而我们也确实如此。

I am inspired every day when I arrive for work. The entrance opens onto the tea room, the heart of my lab. Students in my lab come from many countries and have diverse backgrounds. And they all gather around the tea table every day at 3:30. Some of the most important scientific ideas from my lab arose from tea time discussions.
每天来到工作场所时我都会感到受到启发。入口通向茶室，这是我实验室的核心。我的实验室学生来自不同国家，背景多样，他们每天都会在3点半聚集在茶桌旁。我实验室中一些最重要的科学想法正是源于茶歇时的讨论。

The ceilings in my lab are 10 feet high. Studies have shown that people think and act differently in environments with high ceilings. They think more freely and abstractly. People in a room with low ceilings are more likely to focus on the specifics.
实验室的天花板高达10英尺。研究表明，高天花板的环境会让人以更自由、抽象的方式思考和行动。而在低天花板的房间里，人们更倾向于关注细节。

All of these factors are important for building a community of passionate and creative researchers. Now let's walk into a hospital.
所有这些因素都对建立一个充满热情和创造力的研究社区至关重要。现在让我们走进一间医院。

They are remarkably similar in their layout in every city and almost all countries. In 2004, I attended a workshop in Woods Hole, Massachusetts sponsored by the Academy of Neuroscience for Architecture that explored the design of health care facilities. The workshop was an eye opener. Based on what we know about environments that promote health and healing, modern hospitals could not be more badly designed. Look! First, let's look at the lighting.
各地的医院在布局上惊人地相似，无论在各个城市还是几乎所有国家。2004年，我参加了在马萨诸塞州伍兹霍尔由神经科学建筑学会举办的研讨会，探讨医疗设施的设计。这次研讨会让我大开眼界。根据我们对促进健康和疗愈环境的认知，现代医院的设计可谓极其不合理。请看！首先，我们来看看照明。

Many studies have shown that lighting has a pervasive effect on physiology and behavior. Outdoor light promotes arousal. Dark indoor lighting provokes inactivity. Large windows with views of nature encourage healing. Rooms with small windows overlooking parking lots are depressing.
许多研究表明，照明对生理和行为有着深远的影响。户外光线能促进兴奋，而昏暗的室内光则会引发懒散情绪。大窗户并能看到自然景观的房间有助于康复，而仅能看到停车场的小窗户则令人压抑。

The sickest patients are sent to the intensive care unit, where there are no windows, and the light is kept at the same level all day long.
病情最严重的患者会被送到重症监护室，但那里没有窗户，光线全天保持在同一水平。

We have a circadian clock that regulates awake and sleep cycles, which are entrained by bright light.
我们体内有一个昼夜节律时钟，调节着醒睡周期，并受明亮光线的同步。当你前往一个不同的时区时，会感到迷失，直到昼夜节律重新调整。

When you travel to a distant time zone you feel disoriented for days until your circadian clock has shifted.
让患者处于恒定的光照环境中，会使大脑失去时间感，对昼夜毫无概念。

Putting a patient into constant lighting untethers their brain, making it clueless about the time of day.
声音也是疗愈环境中的重要一部分。医院中的警报可以在任何时间响起，以提醒工作人员注意突发事件。

Sound is also an important part of the healing environment. Alarms can go off any time of the day or night in a hospital to alert the staff of an emergency.
声音也是疗愈环境中的重要因素。在医院中，警报随时可能响起，提醒工作人员应对紧急情况。

This also alarms the brains of the sick people who have enough to worry about without a menacing sound.
然而，这种警报声也会惊扰患者的大脑，使本已忧虑重重的他们更加紧张。

Good nutrition is essential to building a strong body and healthy brain.I have eaten some of the worst food ever in hospitals, unhealthy choices, poorly prepared, go figure. Finally and above all, it is stressful to live in an unpredictable environment. The privacy of hospital patients can be invaded any time of the day or night for an examination, a teaching moment, a blood draw, or even more invasive procedures. If you were worried about your health before arriving at a hospital, the environment there will amplify your worries.
良好的营养对构建强健的身体和健康的大脑至关重要。但我在医院里吃过最差的食物，既不健康又准备得粗糙，令人无语。最后，最重要的是，在不可预测的环境中生活会带来压力。医院里的患者隐私随时可能被打扰——可能是为了检查、教学、抽血，甚至更具侵入性的操作。如果在入院前您已经为健康担忧，那么医院的环境将加剧您的忧虑。
Look around you and notice your environment.Is it conducive to your goals? Is there a way that it can be changed? Sometimes, just changing your walking route can brighten your surroundings.
环顾四周，留意您的环境。它是否有助于您的目标？是否有办法进行改变？有时，仅仅改变步行路线就能让周围环境更令人愉悦。



### 1-10 The Changing World – Matching Aspirations with Opportunity

Horses have been horses the way we know them today for hundreds of thousands of years. In previous evolutionary times, people just ate them, along with buffalo and pretty much any other big game they came across. But about 6,000 years ago, when people figured out that horses could actually be ridden instead of just eaten, the human world started changing dramatically. Suddenly, people could get from place to place a lot more easily, a lot more easily. And this had profound effects on the development of human societies. For example, in the late Middle Ages, the Mongol Empire grew to be one of the largest empires in history, in part because of the tough, scruffy Mongolian horses they used in battle. This is why some argue that the simple idea of the stirrup on a horse's saddle, which gave much greater stability for the rider, was as important an invention as the printing press. We can see the power of the horse even in the recent centuries.
马作为我们今天所认识的模样，已经存在了数十万年。在更早的进化时期，人类只是把马当作食物，与野牛及其他大型猎物一起食用。但大约6000年前，人们发现马不仅可以吃，还可以骑，这一发现极大地改变了人类的世界。突然之间，人们可以更轻松地从一个地方到达另一个地方，而这对人类社会的发展产生了深远的影响。例如，在中世纪晚期，蒙古帝国成为历史上最大的帝国之一，部分原因在于他们在战斗中使用了坚韧、粗犷的蒙古马。这就是为什么有人认为马鞍上的马镫这一简单发明具有与印刷术同等重要的地位，因为它为骑手提供了更大的稳定性。甚至在近几个世纪中，我们仍然能看到马匹的强大影响力。

In the 1700s for example, in the Midwestern plains of the United States, an extraordinary people came into prominence, the Native American group known as the Comanche. They commanded vast swathes of land in what is now Texas, New Mexico, Colorado, and Oklahoma. The Comanche took the culture of the horse to one of the highest levels in human history. Their equine expertise astonished all who knew them. They could outride pretty much anyone, hooking their legs across their ponies' backs and firing arrows under their ponies' necks, keeping their enemies at bay, while using their horse as a protective cover. In the 1700's in fact, all around the world, there were a myriad of jobs that related to horses. Blacksmiths were needed for horses' shoes and to fashion stirrups and bits. There was a demand for wagon, cart, buggy, plow, and harness makers, riders, drivers, couriers, herders. Even if you weren't making or doing something directly related to horses, you still needed to be able to handle a horse, whether you were a monarch, a mercenary or a minister. Horses were an important part of almost everyone's lives, as important in some ways as the computer or smartphone is today. But of course, eras and cultures change. From the horse, we eventually transitioned to the automobile, and all sorts of technologies begin to arise based on the internal combustion engine, from snowmobiles to bulldozers, to jet aircraft. Communications began to play a powerful role too, with the emergence first of the telegraph, then the telephone, and then television, satellites, and of course the Internet.
例如在18世纪，美国中西部大平原上，一个非凡的民族崛起了——科曼奇族，这是一支原住民部落。他们统治着如今的德克萨斯、新墨西哥、科罗拉多和俄克拉荷马的广大地区。科曼奇族将马文化推到了人类历史的巅峰之一。他们的马术技艺令所有见过他们的人惊叹不已。他们几乎能超越任何对手，将双腿挂在小马背上，甚至能在小马脖子下方射箭，以马匹作为掩护抵挡敌人。事实上，在18世纪，世界各地有着无数与马相关的职业。铁匠为马匹打造马蹄铁、马镫和缰绳；车匠、马车制造者、犁具和马具制造者、骑手、驾车人、信使、牧马人等应运而生。即便不从事与马直接相关的工作，也必须学会驾驭马匹，不论是国王、雇佣兵还是传教士。马在几乎每个人的生活中都占据着重要地位，某些方面的作用可与今日的电脑或智能手机相媲美。但时代与文化总在变化。从马匹开始，我们逐步过渡到汽车，并且基于内燃机的各种技术逐渐出现，从雪地摩托到推土机再到喷气式飞机。与此同时，通信也开始扮演重要角色，先是电报，随后是电话、电视、卫星，当然还有互联网的兴起。

And electrical power and safe, clean sources for that power are still of prime importance. So we're living nowadays in a world that's very unlike that of the 1700s. But what our development sense then shows is that people's abilities to learn new things and to grapple with new ideas, to make mindshifts, are the key element in producing vibrant and creative societies, and in helping people to live to their full potential. It's important to be able to match your aspirations and passions with the opportunities that surround you today, as well as the opportunities that will unfold in the days, months and years to come. You want to look at yourself, your career, your knowledge base, with the same sort of big picture perspective that we've just used to consider the development of human societies over the last few centuries. One idea in particular is especially important, which is that whatever skill set and knowledge base you may already have related to your career, your family and children, or your hobbies, it's vital to keep in mind that we're living in a world where at least some passing knowledge of computers, technology, math and science is as important as the knowledge of horsemanship was in centuries gone by. Everyone needs passion in their lives, things you like and enjoy and are good at, but an important part of what we'll be talking about in this MOOC is broadening your passions. That is, we want to encourage you to explore new directions in learning that might not have felt comfortable for you before. If you're a natural techie type, we want to encourage you to broaden your passions and skill set into non-technical areas. Maybe public speaking, or writing, or art. If you're a non-techie type, we'd like to encourage you to go the other way, towards gathering some analytical and technical skills in your arsenal. Whatever your age and whatever your previous training, you want to be able to be flexible and open, and to be able to change and adapt in today's much more rapidly changing world. Mindshift is a MOOC designed to provide a framework for your change, even as you remain true to yourself.
电力以及安全、清洁的电力来源仍然至关重要。因此，我们生活在一个与18世纪截然不同的世界。但自那时以来的发展历程显示，人们学习新事物、应对新思想、进行思维转变的能力，是建立充满活力和创造力的社会、帮助人们发挥全部潜能的关键因素。能够将自己的抱负和热情与当下周围的机会相匹配，并预见未来几天、几个月甚至几年的机会，这一点十分重要。

你需要以一种大局观来看待自己、职业、知识基础，正如我们刚才所做的那样，审视过去几个世纪中人类社会的发展。有一个特别重要的观点，即无论你目前在职业、家庭、子女或爱好方面具备何种技能和知识基础，记住我们生活在一个至少需要一些计算机、技术、数学和科学常识的世界，就如过去的骑术知识一样重要。每个人生活中都需要激情，追求自己喜欢、擅长的事情，但这门慕课中将重点探讨拓宽你的热情。我们希望鼓励你探索一些你之前可能不太熟悉的新学习方向。

如果你天生擅长技术领域，我们希望你能拓宽热情和技能，进入非技术领域，或许是公共演讲、写作或艺术。如果你并非技术型人才，我们则希望你朝相反方向发展，将一些分析和技术技能加入你的武器库。无论你的年龄或之前的培训背景如何，都需要保持灵活开放，在当今瞬息万变的世界中能够改变和适应。《思维转变》这门慕课旨在提供一个框架，帮助你进行改变，同时保持自我。



### 1-11 Natural Passions, Career Choice, and Gender


We should take a few minutes here to touch on the differences between men and women when it comes to natural passions. A biggie here is the hormone testosterone. Testosterone has lots of effects, especially on guys, because guys have lots more of it. Testosterone makes for mustaches, and maybe a bit more cocky behavior, and all sorts of guy things that I tend to kind of like. Even in the womb, guys generally have lots more testosterone than gals. Okay, here I want to show a chart that reveals how testosterone makes a difference in the development of boys and girls math abilities.
我们在这里花几分钟来探讨男女在天然兴趣方面的差异。一个重要的因素就是激素睾酮。睾酮有很多作用，尤其是对男性，因为男性的睾酮含量比女性高得多。睾酮导致胡须的生长，可能让人更自信，产生各种典型的“男子气概”，这些特点我其实蛮喜欢的。甚至在胎儿时期，男性通常也比女性有更多的睾酮。现在我想展示一张图表，揭示睾酮在男孩和女孩数学能力发展中的影响。

Well,  obviously, there isn't really an effect. Where testosterone does have an effect, at least early on, is in verbal abilities. As infants and children develop, it turns out that testosterone can serve as a sort of developmental drag on verbal abilities. So little boys, who have more testosterone, tend on average to get a bit delayed behind girls in their verbal development. This is part of why girls, on average, are more verbally advanced than boys. Remember, boys and girls are roughly equivalent on average in their math and science skills. But when you start to put things together, on average, a girl can look within herself and her own abilities and say, hey, you know what, I'm kind of better at verbal sorts of things. And it's true. A boy, on the other hand, can look within himself, and say, hey, I'm a little better at math kind of things and that's true too. And all of these happen even though girls and boys have roughly the same basic ability to do math.
显然，睾酮对数学能力并没有明显的影响。睾酮的影响，至少在早期，主要体现在语言能力上。随着婴儿和儿童的发展，睾酮会在一定程度上拖慢语言能力的发育。因此，睾酮更多的小男孩在语言发展上平均稍微落后于小女孩。这也是为什么平均而言，女孩的语言能力比男孩更为先进的一部分原因。
记住，男孩和女孩在数学和科学技能方面平均水平是大致相当的。但综合来看，女孩往往会发现自己在语言方面略有优势，而这也确实如此。而男孩则可能意识到自己在数学方面稍占优势，这同样也是真的。这一切都发生在男孩和女孩拥有大致相同的基本数学能力的前提下。

Keep in mind that this is just an average. Individuals can vary quite a bit. And while boys can catch up later in their verbal development, by then, their self image has already begun to solidify. We often develop passions about what we're really good at. As it turns out, it seems easier for girls to get good at subjects requiring strong verbal skills. For boys, quantitative subjects can seem easier than those involving verbal skills. Remember, again this is even though boys and girls have roughly the same basic abilities to do math and science. Unfortunately, what all this does mean is that girls frequent big advantage. Their more advanced verbal skills can inadvertently also serve as a disadvantage. Because of their early verbal advantage, women can sometimes come to believe that their passions lie in language-oriented areas which accounts for part of the reason that there are a fewer women in the technical and scientific fields. Despite the fact that women, as well as men, are strongly needed in those fields. Passions develop about what we're good at but some things take longer for us to get good at. In fact, research has shown that if something seems hard for us, we can actually learn it better than if it was straightforward and easy. All of this can have a bearing on what career paths we tend to choose, especially when we hear advice like, follow your passion which is often taken to mean, do what comes easiest for you. In the discussion forum, describe what you've done or plan to do to broaden your passions in learning, going beyond what you feel you're naturally good at. You can help inspire us all. I'm Barbara Oakley, happy Mindshift.
请记住，这只是一个平均趋势，个体差异可能很大。尽管男孩在语言发展上可以在之后赶上，但那时他们的自我形象已经开始定型了。我们往往对自己擅长的事情更容易产生热情。结果来看，女孩更容易擅长需要较强语言技能的科目，而男孩在定量科目上可能比语言相关的科目更得心应手。再提醒一次，即便如此，男孩和女孩在数学和科学的基本能力上还是相近的。

不幸的是，这一切也意味着女孩的一个显著优势——更高的语言能力，有时会无意中成为劣势。由于她们早期的语言优势，女性有时会认为自己的兴趣在语言相关领域，这也是为什么技术和科学领域中的女性相对较少的部分原因，尽管这些领域迫切需要男女共同参与。热情往往建立在我们擅长的领域之上，但有些技能需要更长时间才能变得擅长。

事实上，研究表明，若某项内容对我们来说困难，我们的学习效果反而会比简单易学的内容更好。这一切都可能影响我们选择的职业道路，特别是当我们听到“追随你的热情”这样的建议时，通常会理解为“选择你最容易的事情去做”。在讨论区中，欢迎大家分享你们为拓宽学习兴趣而做的或计划做的努力，超越你们觉得自己天生擅长的领域，启发我们所有人。



### 1-12 Week 1 Wrap Up

We've had a busy week here in Mindshift. Here are a few of the key points we covered. First, it's okay to be a slow learner. In fact slow learners can some times catch errors that even geniuses miss. Precisely because they're looking at things more slowly and carefully. And as research on mastery learning shows. If you're a slow learner, it may take you more time to learn something, but you can absolutely grasp the material just as well as, and sometimes even better than, fast, racecar brain learners. We also learned how important it is to take active steps yourself in whatever you are learning. Watching a video or reading a book forms a great start to learning and sometimes it's true. That's all you need. But if you are learning something you want to be able to do yourself, whether it's coding, math, speaking another language, learning to dance. Or anything else, you need to actively practice yourself if you want to develop any sort of expertise. Just watching someone solve a problem or speak another language, for example, isn't enough.
在《思维转变》的这一周，我们度过了忙碌的一周，以下是我们讨论的一些关键点。首先，做个慢速学习者完全没问题。事实上，慢速学习者有时能够发现天才们都可能遗漏的错误，正是因为他们以更慢、更仔细的方式观察事物。正如掌握性学习的研究表明，如果您是慢速学习者，虽然学习某些内容可能需要更多时间，但您绝对可以掌握材料，甚至有时比那些快速的“赛车”大脑学习者更好。

我们还了解到在学习中主动参与的重要性。观看视频或阅读书籍是学习的良好开端，有时确实是您所需要的全部。但如果您在学习想要亲自掌握的技能，比如编程、数学、外语、舞蹈或其他任何内容，那么如果想培养某种专业水平，就需要自己主动练习。仅仅看别人解决问题或讲另一种语言是不够的。

Career wise, we learned that your past can have great value for your future even if it seems completely disconnected. So don't feel bad if your past at first.
在职业方面，我们了解到即使过去的经历看似与未来毫无关联，它依然对未来有巨大价值。所以，即便一开始觉得自己的过去没有帮助，也不要因此感到失落。

We looked at focused versus diffuse ways of thinking. And we discovered that slight occasional disruptions as when you're studying in a coffee shop can enhance your ability to learn more difficult big picture sorts of concepts. Music isn't necessarily bad, although it's perhaps best to stick to music without lyrics that isn't too loud. And we found that environment can make a surprising difference in how you feel. We also got an overview of the changing nature of today's world, and the important role of not only language and culture, but of technology. Sometimes your natural passions can fool you. It's important to take a strategic as well as a passion influenced approach to your learning. Next week we'll be getting deeper into happy learning. See you soon!
我们探讨了专注和发散思维方式的区别，发现偶尔的轻微干扰（如在咖啡馆学习时）能够增强学习复杂、大局观概念的能力。音乐并非一定有害，最好选择没有歌词且音量适中的音乐。同时我们了解到，环境会对人的情绪产生意想不到的影响。我们还概览了当今世界的变化特性，不仅语言和文化扮演着重要角色，技术同样重要。

有时，天生的兴趣也可能误导我们，因此在学习中采取既有激情驱动又具战略性的方式非常重要。下周，我们将更深入地探讨快乐学习。期待再见！



### Optional Video: How to Integrate a Flashcard Retrieval Practice System into This Course

When you're trying to learn, your tendency can be to reread what you're learning, or highlight, or underline. But the best way to learn involves an approach called "retrieval practice." You may already know this term. But to better explain it, let's back up a moment to see what happens in your brain when you're learning.
当你尝试学习时，往往会倾向于反复阅读、做标记或划线。然而，最有效的学习方法是一种叫做“提取练习”的方法。你可能已经听说过这个术语。但为了更好地解释它，我们先回顾一下当你学习时大脑中发生了什么。

When you learn something—anything— you are creating links between neurons in long-term memory. Each time you retrieve these ideas from your own mind, you're strengthening those sets of links. Hundreds of studies have shown that retrieval practice— retrieving the ideas from your own mind—is the best approach to learning. It not only builds memory but also significantly boosts your conceptual understanding. What's the best way to do retrieval practice? Use flashcards, repeating your practice over a number of days until you can retrieve the ideas, approaches, or techniques easily from your own mind.
当你学习某件事时——无论是什么——你都在长时记忆中创建神经元之间的连接。每次从自己脑中提取这些想法时，都是在加强这些连接。数百项研究表明，提取练习（即从自己脑中提取想法）是最佳的学习方法。它不仅能增强记忆，还能显著提升对概念的理解。

那么，进行提取练习的最佳方式是什么呢？使用闪卡（记忆卡），在数天内反复练习，直到你能轻松从脑中提取出这些想法、方法或技术。

Let's take a couple of minutes and show you how you can use an app called iDoRecall to create spaced repetition flashcards right here on Coursera as you take this course. First, go to iDoRecall.com to create a free account. Sign up here and verify your email. After you click the link in that email, you'll be taken inside iDoRecall for the first time. Next, go to the integration section in your profile and add the web clipper browser extension. The extension works only in Chrome-based browsers, and Microsoft Edge. Pin the extension here so that you can click on it whenever you need to activate it.
我们来花几分钟展示如何在Coursera上使用名为iDoRecall的应用来创建间隔重复的闪卡。首先，前往iDoRecall.com创建一个免费账号。注册并验证你的电子邮件，点击邮件中的链接后，将第一次进入iDoRecall。

接着，进入你的个人资料的集成部分，添加网页剪辑浏览器扩展。该扩展仅在基于Chrome的浏览器和Microsoft Edge中运行。将扩展固定在工具栏上，以便随时点击激活它。

Now you're ready to rock and roll with retrieving! After you watch one of the videos in our course, take a minute to skim the transcript. Pick out the key points that you want to remember and create a note by highlighting the text. Later on, head over to your notes section for the course. IDoRecall enables you to create spaced repetition flashcards that are linked to highlights or any subsequent notes that you add to enrich the highlights. Select some text. Create your flash card. We call these flashcards "recalls." Back in your free iDoRecall account, you can see the recall that we just created.
现在，你已经准备好开始“提取”练习了！观看完课程视频后，花一分钟浏览一下文字记录，挑选你想记住的要点，并通过高亮文本创建一条笔记。之后，前往课程的笔记部分。iDoRecall允许你创建与高亮或后续添加的补充笔记链接的间隔重复闪卡。

选择一些文本，创建你的闪卡，我们称这些闪卡为“recalls”。回到你的免费iDoRecall账户，你将看到刚刚创建的recall。

Let's practice memory retrieval. Read the question and do your best to retrieve the answer from your memory. By doing this, you're strengthening the synaptic connections between neurons and making it easier to retrieve and understand the memory, concept, or skill in the future. Now check the answer. If you struggled with the recall, click to see the source link. Your Coursera notes will open in a new browser tab.
让我们练习记忆提取。阅读问题，并尽量从记忆中提取答案。通过这样做，你在加强神经元之间的突触连接，使得未来更容易提取和理解这些记忆、概念或技能。现在检查答案。如果你在回忆时遇到困难，可以点击查看来源链接，你的Coursera笔记将会在新标签页中打开。

Click here to view the relevant portion of the video and refresh your memory in the context where you learned it. Each day, the iDoRecall spaced repetition algorithm will schedule for practice the small subset of the recalls that you're getting closest to forgetting. iDoRecall recall is free for the first 200 recalls in your account. This should be enough to both get you through the course and show you the power of a good flashcard system.
点击此处查看视频的相关部分，在原学习情境中刷新你的记忆。每天，iDoRecall的间隔重复算法会安排即将被遗忘的部分recall进行练习。在你的账户中，iDoRecall的前200张recall卡片是免费的。这应该足够你完成课程，同时体验到一个优秀闪卡系统的强大之处。



### Optional Bonus Video: Nelson Dellis Memory Tips #1

Hello, how you doing? My name's Nelson Dellis. What I'm going to do is show you how to memorize something you can use right now. Let's go.  All right, so what we're going to learn today, I'm going to make you make you memorize ten seemingly pretty random things. Cool?  Cool. The first thing we're going to do is we're going to choose ten body parts. We're going to start at the top of our head, that's going to be the first one. Then we're going to come down to our ears, that's two. Our eyes, this area, three. The nose, four. The mouth, five. The chin, six. The armpit, seven. Belly button chest area eight. The knees nine. And then down on the floor on my feet will be ten. Very simple. So, first things first, we're at the top of our head. I want you to picture some chopsticks.  There's some chopsticks on the top of my head, kind of picking out some sushi, whatever. There's two wooden sticks stuck in my hair. Okay, next, we're down to the ears, all right. Delicious Indian curry dish.
你好，最近怎么样？我叫尼尔森·德利斯。我将向你展示一个可以马上使用的记忆方法。准备好了吗？好，我们今天要学习的内容是，我会让你记住十个看似随机的东西，感觉如何？酷吧？首先，我们会选择十个身体部位。
我们从头顶开始，这是第一个。接下来是耳朵，第二个。眼睛区域，第三个。鼻子，第四个。嘴巴，第五个。下巴，第六个。腋下，第七个。肚脐或胸部区域，第八个。膝盖，第九个。然后到地面上的脚，第十个。非常简单。
那么，首先，我们从头顶开始。我希望你想象一些筷子，有些筷子在我的头顶，似乎在夹寿司之类的东西。有两根木筷插在我的头发里。好，接下来是耳朵，一个美味的印度咖喱菜。

Now really imagine you can taste it. It's overflowing, coming out of my ears. The actual sauce is hot and smells delicious you want to eat it, it's coming out of my ears.  Next we got a hamburger.  And that's going to go on the eyes. And imagine a burger is coming out of your eyes or that there's a burger blocking your eye. The juices from the beef coming out of your eye dripping down your cheek like a tear of beef sauce. All right, next, we're at the nose. We have this person putting a palm into the dough. So I want you to remember in dough.  Into the dough, in dough. And so imagine that's happening on my nose, so you're going to imagine someone pressing dough into my nose.
现在，真的想象一下你能尝到它的味道。酱汁溢出来了，从我的耳朵里流出来。酱汁是热的，闻起来很美味，让你想吃掉它，它从我的耳朵里流出来。接下来是汉堡。它要放在眼睛上。想象一个汉堡从你的眼睛里冒出来，或者有个汉堡挡住了你的眼睛。牛肉汁从你的眼睛里流出，像牛肉汁的泪滴一样顺着脸颊流下来。好吧，接下来是鼻子。有人把手掌按进面团中，我想让你记住“面团中”。按进面团，面团中。所以想象这发生在我的鼻子上，你要想象有人把面团压进我的鼻子里。

All right, the next one, on the mouth, we're going to picture a bra.  I want you to imagine a bra is just coming out of my mouth. I've got it deep down in my throat, I'm just like pulling it out and there it is, a bra coming out of my mouth.
好吧，接下来是嘴巴。我希望你想象一副胸罩。想象一下，一副胸罩正从我的嘴里出来。我深深地把它吞下，正拉出来，那里就是，一副胸罩从我的嘴里冒出来。

We move down to the chin. I want you to picture a pack of cards just resting on my chin.  And there's a dealer who wants to just deal these cards, these individual cards from the pack off of my chin. So that goes right there. So we've done the whole face. The next thing I want you to picture is that I lift my armpit, and splurting out of my armpit are a bunch of Cheerio type cereal. Boom.  Next, we're down at the bellybutton right. I want you to imagine that out of my bellybutton is a gun just firing all sorts of lint and gross bellybutton stuff out at you. And what sound does it make? Bang, right? Bang.  Imagine that right here, bang. So down here on the knees. Let's just imagine that we have a bunch of martini and vodka on the knees.  So this knee is the vodka and I pour it into this glass here, somehow like that. And now down to the feet. I have boots on, why not, because that's memorable. Down on the floor is a pan.  It's cooking something off of the floor, and it smells delicious. You just imagine the pan on the floor. You got all that? I hope so, and I want you to take a few minutes just to see if you can remember, starting at my head, and down to my feet, what the ten things were. I'm going to go do something while you do that. I'll be right back.  All right, that should've given you enough time to review everything. Let's go over them one more time just to see if you got them. First thing was the chopsticks on my head.
我们往下到下巴。我希望你想象一副扑克牌正静静地放在我的下巴上。有个发牌员想从我的下巴上发出这些牌。就这样。现在我们已经完成了整个面部。接下来，我希望你想象我抬起腋下，很多像麦圈那样的谷物正从我的腋下喷出来。砰！然后我们来到肚脐。我希望你想象，从我的肚脐里有一把枪正在发射各种绒毛和肚脐里的脏东西。它发出什么声音？砰，对吧？砰！想象就在这里，砰！接下来是膝盖。我们想象膝盖上有一堆马提尼和伏特加。这只膝盖是伏特加，我把它倒进这只杯子里，就像这样。最后，我们来到脚。我穿着靴子，为什么不呢，因为这样更容易记住。地上有一个锅，锅里正在煮东西，闻起来很美味。想象一下那个锅就在地上。你记住这些了吗？希望如此。我希望你花几分钟回顾一下，从我的头到我的脚，回想起这十样东西。我去做点事情，等你完成。我马上回来。好吧，应该给你足够的时间来复习所有内容了。让我们再过一遍，看看你是否记得。首先是头上的筷子。

Out of my ear was the chicken curry the Indian dish. On my eye was a hamburger. Out of my nose was some dough that I was pushing in. Out of my mouth was a very long and sexy bra.
从我的耳朵里流出的咖喱鸡是印度菜。从我的眼睛上是汉堡。从我的鼻子里是我正在压入的面团。从我的嘴里则是一副非常长的性感胸罩。

Out of my chin there was a dealer dealing out cards from a pack of cards. Then we go to my armpits. What was coming out of it? Cheerios. Out of my bellybutton, bang, like a gun. On my knees, martini glass with vodka in it. And then finally, on the floor the pan cooking stuff by my funky boots.
从我的下巴上有个发牌员正在发放扑克牌。从我的腋下流出的是什么？麦圈（Cheerios）。从我的肚脐里，砰，像枪一样的声音。膝盖上则是一个盛着伏特加的马提尼杯。最后，地上有一个锅，旁边是我时尚的靴子，锅里正在煮东西。

### 2-1 Week 2 Introduction



This week, we're going to be doing a little brain excavation to learn that focusing intently isn't always the best thing for optimal learning and mind shifting. We'll discover other valuable methods to enhance your learning. And we'll see some unbelievable feats people can accomplish using these techniques. We'll also warn you about popular learning approaches that research has shown are actually harmful. Perhaps most importantly, we'll give you tools to help slip past your inner procrastinator and other proven tools to help you reframe and reduce stress. All this and more in this week's Mindshift.
本周，我们将进行一些“脑部挖掘”，了解专注并不总是最有利于最佳学习和思维转变的方式。我们将发现其他有价值的方法来增强你的学习效果，并看到人们使用这些技巧所能取得的令人难以置信的成就。我们还会提醒你一些流行的学习方法，这些方法经过研究证明其实是有害的。

也许最重要的是，我们将提供一些工具，帮助你克服内心的拖延症，并提供其他经过验证的工具来帮助你重塑思维、减轻压力。所有这些内容以及更多精彩内容，都在本周的《思维转变》中。



### 2-2 The Value of a Poor Memory


We sometimes fool ourselves about our good attributes with relation to learning. We think they're bad. We saw that with the race car brains verses the hiker brains. Being a slow hiker type thinker can give you an advantage because you can be less likely to jump to conclusions and more able to flexibly change your mind when you're wrong.
我们有时会对自己在学习方面的优点产生误解，认为它们是缺点。我们在“赛车大脑”和“徒步大脑”的对比中看到了这一点。作为一个慢速的徒步思考者，实际上可以让你更具优势，因为你不容易仓促下结论，并且在错误时更能灵活地调整自己的想法。

But there's another advantageous attribute that we often think is bad, and that is a poor memory. The reality is that a poor memory has a valuable side to it. Let's review the key idea. We know that the prefrontal cortex has roughly four slots of working memory. This means we can hold a maximum of about four neural chunks of information in those slots when we focus our attention. Chunks can be simple, like those related to numbers, the notes that form a musical chord, or words or phrases in a foreign language. If we've practiced enough, we can build more complex neural chunks that we can easily pull into working memory and work with. The neural chunks are kind of like ribbons of thought. These neural chunks can also relate to more complicated ideas, like longer portions of a song, or a more complex equation. As you can see, these neural sort of chunks, ribbons, can, with practice, involve incredibly complex activities. Experts have lots of well practiced neural chunks that they can easily bring to mind. The key idea here is that when you first look at something to try to figure it out, your working memory in your prefrontal cortex is working very hard. But once you've understood that something and practiced enough with it to form a solid neural pattern, you've created a neural chunk. That's like that ribbon that you can easily draw into one slot of working memory, leaving the rest of your working memory free for other processing. And this is why, once we've practiced enough, beginning since we were toddlers, as adults, we can think a single thought, like walk towards the door, and walk, which is actually a very complex maneuver, without even continuing to think about it. Some people have working memories like steel traps, whatever ideas come before them can be easily retained in their working memories. This can make it easier for them to understand complex topics and solve complicated problems. But other people, like say, me, have not so good working memories. They may get something in mind, but then, shiny, they get distracted, and then some of what they were thinking about falls out their minds. But when that something falls out, something else comes in. And that's where creativity comes to play. Those new ideas that come willy nilly into your mind can be the source of creative new thoughts. And in fact, as we know from research, those with poor working memories are often more creative. Do you have to work harder to keep up with the steel trap memory types? Sure, but you wouldn't want to trade the asset that your poor memory gives you, that is your creativity. But digging deeper, a poor working memory means something else. As it turns out, a poor working memory gives you an effective tool to figure out simpler ways to do things. It may take you a while to figure those things out, but when you do figure them out, you can sometimes see elegant simplifications and brilliant shortcuts  that a person with a strong working memory just doesn't have the motivation to figure out. There's more. Andrew Wiles, for example, is a mathematical legend who, after 358 years of efforts by some of the world's leading mathematicians, at last proved Fermat's last theorem, has pointed out that you often need forgetting, as well as remembering, to help you solve problem.
